# 71:1
## b9md
See [[PSA-b9md]]
## lb3w
Going to Yahweh for protection is spoken of as taking refuge in him. Alternate translation: “go to you, Yahweh, for protection” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ew7k
This can be stated in active form. See how “do not let me be humiliated” is translated in [Psalms 25:2](../025/002.md). Alternate translation: “never let my enemies put me to shame” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 71:2
## xl47
Possible meanings are (1) “make me safe because you always do what is right” or (2) “make me safe as I do what you want me to do” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## q2dk
Here “your ear” refers to God’s willingness to hear someone who prays to him. See how this is translated in [Psalms 17:6](../017/006.md). Alternate translation: “pay attention to me” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## qwt1
“keep me safe”

# 71:3
## a39u
The psalmist requests that Yahweh make him as safe as he would be if he were hiding in a large rock or cliff where his enemies could not find him. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## w8pz
These are hills or mountains, not rocks one can hold in one’s hand.

## usz4
“you have commanded your angels”

## xd9e
The psalmist believes that Yahweh will protect him and make him as safe as if he were hiding on top of a large mountain or inside a man-made fortress. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 71:4
## p4mg
Possible meanings are (1) the word “hand” is a metonym for power. Alternate translation: “from the power of the wicked, from the power of the unrighteous” or (2) “hand” refers to the person himself. Alternate translation: “from wicked people, from unrighteous … people” or “so that wicked people and unrighteous … people cannot harm me” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## pb9a
“wicked; rescue me out of the hand of the unrighteous.” You can make clear the understood information. (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## pdn5
These adjectives can be translated as noun phrases. Alternate translation: “wicked people … unrighteous people … cruel people” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

# 71:5
## tph7
Here “hope” is a metonym for the one in whom the psalmist hopes. Alternate translation: “you are the one whom I confidently expect to help me” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 71:6
## rmx7
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “You have supported me from the womb” or “You have taken care of me ever since I came out of my mother’s womb” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## h427
“you are the one”

# 71:7
## cq9t
“Many people see how I live and want to live as I do”

# 71:8
## di1q
The psalmist speaks of his desire to praise and honor Yahweh with the words he speaks as if his mouth were full of words the way it can be full of food. Alternate translation: “My mouth will be filled with words that praise you … that honor you” or “I will always praise you … will always honor you” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## vh1s
Here “praise” refer to the words that he will use to praise Yahweh. Alternate translation: “words that tell people how great you are” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## dq42
Here “honor” refer to the words that he will use to honor Yahweh. Alternate translation: “words that cause people to honor you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## z7sk
“all day long” or “at all times”

# 71:9
## hwz9
These two phrases have basically the same meaning and are used together for emphasis. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## ve6g
“Do not force me to go away from you.” For God to reject him is spoken of as God forcing him to go away. See how this is translated in [Psalms 51:11](../051/011.md). Alternate translation: “do not reject me” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## l9jz
“do not leave me forever”

# 71:10
## wp8v
It is implied that they are watching for an opportunity to take his life. The euphemism “to take a life” means to kill. Alternate translation: “watch for an opportunity to take my life” or “wait for an opportunity to kill me” (See: [[figs-explicit]] and [[figs-euphemism]])

# 71:11
## kar6
This can be translated to make explicit that they are talking about him. Alternate translation: “They say about me” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## fv3j
“follow him when he runs away, and kill him”

# 71:12
## acn7
Because Yahweh has not yet answered the writer’s request, he speaks of Yahweh as if Yahweh were standing far away from the writer. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## eu8k
“help me soon”

# 71:13
## fkp7
“Let those who are hostile to my life be put to shame and destroyed”

## zj77
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Put them to shame and destroy them” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## m9nd
The words “my life” represent the person. The idiom “hostile to my life” means that these are people who try to harm him by accusing him of wrongdoing. Alternate translation: “those who accuse me of doing wrong” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-idiom]])

## jq5a
“let those who seek my hurt be covered with rebuke and dishonor”

## bfc1
This can be stated in active form. The abstract nouns “rebuke” and “dishonor” can be stated as verbs. Alternate translation: “may everyone rebuke them, and may no one honor them” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## i5m7
The noun “hurt” can be translated with a verb. Alternate translation: “those who are looking for ways to harm me” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 71:14
## ew9i
“more all the time” or “always more than I have before”

# 71:15
## ukx5
“I do not completely understand your righteousness and your salvation, but my mouth will tell about them all the day”

## r4ct
The mouth is synecdoche for the whole person. Alternate translation: “I will tell” or “I will speak with my mouth and tell” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## diu6
This can be restated to remove the abstract noun “righteousness.” Alternate translation: “how righteous you are” or “all the good things you do” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## hjv1
The abstract noun “salvation” can be stated as an action. Alternate translation: “how you have saved me” or “how you save people” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 71:16
## mem9
Possible meanings are (1) “I will go to where people worship Yahweh” or (2) “I will go to my enemies” (See: [[figs-go]])

## ntp3
Possible meanings are (1) “I will tell them of the mighty deeds the Lord Yahweh has done” or (2) “because the Lord Yahweh has given me strength to do mighty acts.”

## xcy8
“will talk about”

# 71:17
## af7a
The implied information may be stated clearly. “taught me many things” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 71:18
## u6gc
“God, please do not forsake me”

## u45h
The abstract noun “strength” can be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “I have been telling how strong you are” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## bs2s
“to those who are children today”

## k6sb
The omitted information may be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “and as I declare your power to everyone who is to come” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## x4ym
This exaggeration refers to everyone the writer can speak to. Alternate translation: “so as many people as I can tell will know” (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

# 71:19
## mu1p
The psalmist speaks of the good things God has done as though they were put together as a high building or a mountain. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## a913
This rhetorical question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “there is no one like you!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 71:20
## wi74
strengthen or make lively again

## sf33
“The depths of the earth” here is a metaphor for where people go when they die. The psalmist had not already died, but in this exaggeration he speaks as if he had. Alternate translation: “when we are near death” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-hyperbole]])

# 71:21
## b5t3
“I want you to increase … I want you to turn again and comfort.” Some translations read, “You will increase … you will turn again and comfort.”

## e1ew
This describes one action through two phrases. Alternate translation: “comfort me again” (See: [[figs-hendiadys]])

# 71:22
## ix4p
“to you, who is the Holy One of Israel, I will sing praises while I play the harp”

# 71:23
## aku6
“Lips” represent the whole person. Alternate translation: “I will shout for joy” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## we4f
The words the psalmist has omitted can be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “and my soul, which you have redeemed, will sing praises” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## ijs7
Here “soul” refers to the whole person. (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 71:24
## wy5p
Here “tongue” refers to the whole person. Alternate translation: “I will also talk” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## mf6u
“those who sought my hurt have been put to shame and are confused”

## h4mq
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “for God has put to shame and confused them” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## kz4y
This refers to the enemies of the writer. ([Psalms 71:10](../071/010.md)). See how “those who seek my hurt” is translated in [Psalms 71:13](./013.md).

# 72:intro
## h1hi
See [[PSA-h1hi]]
# 72:1
## u47g
See [[PSA-u47g]]
## sep9
Possible meanings are (1) “Give me, the king, your righteous decrees, God, your righteousness to my son” or (2) “Give me, the king, your righteous decrees, God, your righteousness to me, the king’s son.” People in those days would often speak of themselves as if they were someone else. However, it would be best to translate this as though the psalmist is speaking of someone else, not of himself. (See: [[figs-123person]])

## vw1m
“Enable the king to judge rightly”

## p2xg
The verb may be supplied from the previous phrase. Here the word “righteousness” refers to making righteous decisions. Alternate translation: “give your righteousness to the king’s son” or “enable the king’s son to rule with righteousness” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

# 72:2
## d3dt
If David wrote this, he is talking about his son, “the king’s son,” he is speaking of the time when his son will be king. If Solomon wrote it, even though he is writing about himself, it would be best to translate as though he were writing about someone else. Either way, “May the king judge” is the best translation.

## d51c
The psalmist is speaking to God.

## faw9
The verb may be supplied from the previous phrase. The adjective “poor” refers to poor people. Alternate translation: “may he judge your poor people” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-nominaladj]])

# 72:3
## fx1z
The psalmist speaks of the people of Israel as if they were the mountains and hills on which they live. He speaks of the mountains and the hills as if they were the entire land of Israel, as if that land were a garden that produces fruit, and of peace and righteousness as if they are that fruit. Alternate translation: “May the people of the land live in peace … may they do everything in a righteous way” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 72:4
## ln6a
These refer to the one who will “judge” ([Psalms 72:2](../072/002.md)).

## lx1g
The writer speaks of the king defeating or punishing people who oppress others as if those people were objects that the king would break into pieces. Alternate translation: “punish the person who oppresses others” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 72:5
## qf1t
The sun and the moon are metonyms for the day and the night, which together are a merism for all time. Alternate translation: “forever, and without ending” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-merism]])

# 72:6
## fay4
The king will be good, and he will do good things for his people as if he were rain doing good for freshly cut grass. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## bsl9
“I desire that he come”

## v3st
“May he come down like showers that water.” The king will be good, and he will do good things for his people as if he were rain doing good for the ground. (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-simile]])

# 72:7
## ch52
The adjective “righteous” can be translated as a noun phrase. Alternate translation: “righteous people” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

## pqr9
Possible meanings are (1) “while the king rules” or (2) “as long as the righteous person lives” or “as long as the righteous people live.”

## r4e2
The psalmist speaks as if peace were a physical object like food. An “abundance” is when there is much of something. This can be restated to remove the abstract nouns “abundance” and “peace.” Alternate translation: “may righteous people live peacefully” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## m2kd
“as long as the moon shines” or “forever” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 72:8
## mm24
“May the king have dominion”

## vh7b
Both of these expressions are merisms and refer to the whole earth. (See: [[figs-merism]])

## v5fy
from the Dead Sea and the Sea of Kinnereth in the east to the Mediterranean Sea in the west.

## m3b5
“the Euphrates River,” which the Israelites would travel to on land by going north

## dxj8
as far as people could travel on land by different routes to the south. The Israelites spoke of the earth as if it were a flat surface with ends. (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 72:9
## c1h1
This is a metaphor for extreme humiliation. Alternate translation: “do everything they can so he will allow them to live” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 72:10
## ds5s
This is the name of a place. (See: [[translate-names]])

## jht5
pay

## h88r
“give gifts”

## pg7c
This is the name of a country. It is not the same country as Sheba. (See: [[translate-names]])

# 72:11
## j7ze
“bow down before him” or “honor him as their king”

## ivl3
Here the word “nations” represents the people who live in the nations. Alternate translation: “the people who live in every nation” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 72:12
## cj1a
“no one else to help him”

# 72:13
## w2hi
“He wants to stop the poor and needy from suffering”

## dra6
Here the nominal adjectives “poor” and “needy” mean basically the same thing and emphasize that they are unable to help themselves. Alternate translation: “those who are poor and those who are needy” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-nominaladj]])

# 72:14
## iey7
Here “lives” refers to the whole person. Alternate translation: “redeems them” or “saves them” or “rescues them” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## a1wj
These two words mean basically the same thing and emphasize how badly the needy suffer. These abstract nouns can be stated as verbs. Alternate translation: “those who oppress them and hurt them” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## gq3a
Here “their blood” is a metonym for their well-being. The sight of Yahweh represents his judgment or evaluation. Alternate translation: “their well-being is very important to him” or “he wants them to live well” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## u32y
See how this phrase is translated in [Psalms 19:14](../019/014.md).

# 72:15
## e29x
This saying was used to honor the king by expressing a desire to have him live a long time. Alternate translation: “May the king live a long time!” or “I desire that the king live a long time!” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## gew3
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “May they give him the gold of Sheba” or “May he receive the gold of Sheba” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## zya3
This idiom means “continually” and does not refer only to one day. Alternate translation: “continually” or “all the time” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 72:16
## d9j4
An “abundance” is when there is much of something. This abstract noun can be stated as “much” or “plenty” Alternate translation: “much grain” or “plenty of grain” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## nb8u
plants that people grow for food

## d8rg
Use the word for what long grass does when a gentle wind blows on it and it moves slowly back and forth.

## p8u5
“like the cedar trees in Lebanon.” These trees were beautiful and had wood that was good for building. The meaning of this can be made clear. (See: [[figs-simile]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## rl8f
The prosperity of the people in the cities is spoken of as if they were grass that grows abundantly in the fields. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 72:17
## d3jj
This saying is used to honor God by expressing the desire for people to always remember him. Alternate translation: “May people always know about him” or “May people never forget who he is” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## ly6h
Here “his name” refers to God’s reputation. Alternate translation: “the king’s name” or “the king’s reputation” or “the king’s fame” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## ys89
The verb may be supplied from the previous phrase. Alternate translation: “as long as the sun endures” or “as long as the sun shines” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## dq84
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “may God cause him to do good things for people” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## ru3v
“recognize that God has blessed them”

# 72:18
## ti3p
See [[PSA-ti3p]]
## j629
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “May people bless Yahweh God, the God of Israel” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 72:19
## nw8m
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “May people bless his glorious name forever” or, treating “name” as a metonym for Yahweh himself, “May people forever know how glorious he is” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## s32j
“he, who is glorious, be blessed”

## qe7e
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “may his glory fill the whole earth” or “may he fill the whole earth with his glory” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## q3wa
The word “Amen” is repeated to emphasize approval of what has been said. See how you translated this in [Psalms 41:13](../041/013.md). Alternate translation: “May it certainly be so”

# 72:20
## ktc5
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “David, the son of Jesse has finished his prayers” or “This is the last prayer of the David the son of Jesse” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 73:intro
## t2n1
See [[PSA-t2n1]]
# 73:1
## p92k
See [[PSA-p92k]]
# 73:2
## bq59
The psalmist speaks of being unable to trust God and wanting to sin as if he had almost fallen while walking on a slippery path. Alternate translation: “I almost stopped trusting in God; I was almost guilty of committing a great sin against him” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 73:3
## eb9z
“I envied the arrogant” or “I did not want arrogant people to have the good things that they had”

## jit7
The adjective “arrogant” can be translated as a noun phrase. Alternate translation: “arrogant people” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

## k8tb
The word “prosperity” can be translated as a verb. Alternate translation: “how the wicked have so many good things” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## tu1r
The adjective “wicked” can be translated as a noun phrase. Alternate translation: “wicked people” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

# 73:4
## b59h
See [[PSA-b59h]]
# 73:5
## uzu7
The words “burdens” is a metonym for the struggles of daily life (need for food, shelter, clothing, and health). (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## t5qn
“they do not suffer the way other people do”

# 73:6
## kp3l
See [[PSA-kp3l]]
## r8m8
This means the wicked show everyone how proud and violent they are as if they were wearing a necklace or beautiful robe. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## c2uh
This refers to things that rich and important people wear.

## fyb1
a chain usually made of gold or jewels that goes around the neck

# 73:7
## ttd1
Because they are like blind people who cannot see where they are going, they sin without knowing it. Being blind is a metaphor for a person being unable to see how wicked he is. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## l8fr
Here the psalmist describes people’s thoughts as if they were people. He also describes the inner beings of wicked people as if they were a building in which those people could walk. Alternate translation: “in their inner beings they are always thinking about more evil things to do” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 73:8
## alc5
See [[PSA-alc5]]
## fmq4
Whom they mock can be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “They mock God and his people” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 73:9
## b91p
Here “their mouth” is a metonym for their speech, and “heavens” is a metonym for God, who lives in the heavens. Alternate translation: “They speak against God, who is in the heavens” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## cln7
The word “tongues” represents the people themselves. Possible meanings are (1) “they go through the earth saying bad things about God” or (2) “they go everywhere and boast about themselves.” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 73:10
## t3at
See [[PSA-t3at]]
## ack4
Possible meanings are (1) “God’s people love the wicked people” or (2) “the wicked people return to this place”

## k4em
Possible meanings are (1) “God’s people listen gladly to the words of the wicked people” or (2) “the wicked people have plenty of food to eat and wine to drink”

# 73:11
## k4v1
the wicked people say

## iqv8
These rhetorical questions are to show contempt for God. These questions can be translated as statements. Alternate translation: “Surely God does not know what we are doing. The Most High has no knowledge of it.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 73:13
## i3dh
See [[PSA-i3dh]]
## m4jl
The word “I” refers to Asaph.

## l9bt
Asaph speaks of guarding his heart as if he were guarding a city or building against enemies. Alternate translation: “I have kept my thoughts pure” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## v713
The writer speaks of his purity as if he had washed his hands with innocence instead of with water. See how you translated this in [Psalms 26:6](../026/006.md). Alternate translation: “my actions have remained pure” or “I have washed my hands to show that I am innocent” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 73:14
## svu5
“always” or “every day”

## gtr9

## c72g
“I have been … punished”

# 73:15
## c23c
This hypothetical situation did not happen. Alternate translation: “I never said, ‘I will say these things,’ so I did not betray this generation of your children” (See: [[figs-hypo]])

# 73:16
## z5qd
The good things that happen to “the wicked” ([Psalms 73:4](../073/004.md)-[Psalms 12](./010.md)).

# 73:17
## r3rj
“what happens to wicked people when they die” or “how wicked people die”

# 73:18
## n9na
The word “them” refers to the wicked.

## pt49
“unsafe or unstable ground.” See how “slipped” is translated in [Psalms 73:2](../073/002.md).

# 73:19
## b63l
The word “wilderness” is a metaphor for a person who has lost everything good. Alternate translation: “How quickly they are destroyed” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 73:20
## d2qf
The wicked will last no longer than what a person sees in a dream. It disappears as soon as the person wakes up. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 73:21
## dz6z
The word “heart” represents the person emphasizing their thoughts and feelings. Alternate translation: “I was very sad” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## r3jv
The psalmist speaks of emotional pain as if it were physical pain by being pierced with a knife or arrow. Alternate translation: “I felt like someone had wounded me” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 73:22
## ei39
These two phrases mean basically the same thing and emphasize how little he knew. Alternate translation: “very ignorant” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## sb92
“understood nothing”

## xw9q
This “you” refers to God.

# 73:23
## v8cf
The word “I” here represents Asaph. The word “you” here represents God.

## mif5
The person’s “right hand” refers to the whole person. This shows an intimate relationship with God that offers stability and security. Alternate translation: “You hold me close” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 73:24
## yt7j
Possible meanings are (1) “put me where people will honor me” or (2) “honor me by taking me to where you are.” See how “receive me” is translated in [Psalms 49:15](../049/015.md).

# 73:25
## l5a5
You can translate this as a statement. Alternate translation: “There is no one for me in heaven but you!” or “You are the only one I have in heaven!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## p7l4
“among the gods” or “among the supernatural beings”

# 73:26
## u7tm
These two phrases share similar meanings. Together they represent the whole person. Alternate translation: “My body and my mind” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-merism]])

# 73:27
## zd6j
See [[PSA-zd6j]]
## i4nr
Here the idea of staying far from God is compared to being unwilling to obey him. Alternate translation: “Those who do not want to obey you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 73:28
## s5pl
The writer speaks of Yahweh as if he were a place to which a person could flee for safety. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## dt7g

# 74:9
## c4rh

Possible meanings are (1) “We do not see any more miraculous signs from God” or (2) “All our sacred symbols are gone” or (3) the “signs” are the flags and symbols of an army representing the army itself. Alternate translation: “They have completely destroyed our army” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 74:10
## d7ac
This can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “God, the enemy has been throwing insults at you for too long!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## qxe3
This can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “It seems as if you will never stop the enemy from blaspheming your name!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## nv8x
The word “name” is a metonym for God himself. Alternate translation: “say that you are bad” or “insult you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 74:11
## mc4f
The psalmist speaks of God as if he had a physical body. This can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Stop holding back your hand, your right hand!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-personification]])

## m38y
Here the word “hand” is a metonym for power. Alternate translation: “not show your power” or “not use your power to destroy your enemies” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## hk11
“your strongest hand”

## nc4l
The psalmist speaks of God as if he had a physical body. “Stop hiding your power and take action” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-personification]])

# 74:12
## n2ul
See [[PSA-n2ul]]
## r8vz
Possible meanings are (1) Asaph is speaking as Israel’s representative, “God has been our king since we Israelites first became a nation” or (2) “God, my king, was alive even in ancient times.”

## tqm1
The abstract noun “salvation” can be translated with the verb “to save.” Here Yahweh saving people is spoken of as if salvation were an object that he brings with him. Alternate translation: “saving people” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 74:13
## gap9
Asaph is probably speaking of the time God brought Israel out of Egypt, divided the Sea of Reeds, led Israel through it on dry land, then drowned Pharaoh’s army. (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## ag39
Asaph is speaking of God as if God had a physical body. “You are so strong you were able to make dry land in the middle of the sea.” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## r6uh
“the great water”

## f387
Asaph is probably speaking of Pharaoh and his army as if they were sea monsters. If possible, translate these words literally. Alternate translation: “when you killed Pharaoh’s army, it was as if you smashed the heads of sea monsters in the waters” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 74:14
## t17u
See [[PSA-t17u]]
## dzv3
Leviathan is a sea monster. It represents a cruel enemy. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 74:15
## dqs2
Yahweh causing springs and streams to flow out of the ground as if he broke open the ground so that they could flow. Alternate translation: “You caused springs and streams to flow out of the ground” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## i6gg

# 74:18
## c6xs

## g7dq
“Pay attention to.” See how this is translated in [Psalms 74:2](../074/002.md). (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## x7xk
Asaph speaks of insulting words as if they were physical objects, like stones, which the enemy was throwing at Yahweh. Alternate translation: “the enemy insulted you many times” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 74:19
## x5rc
Asaph speaks of himself as if he were a dove, a defenseless bird. This phrase may also be a metaphor for the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “me, your dove” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## zrd5
A small, defenseless bird often kept as a pet.

## tp9a
This phrase may be a metaphor for the enemies of Israel. Alternate translation: “a savage enemy that is like a wild animal” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## rgi5
“Do not continue forever to do nothing to help your oppressed people.” This can be stated in positive form. Alternate translation: “Come soon to help your oppressed people” (See: [[figs-litotes]])

# 74:20
## y6si
See [[PSA-y6si]]
## x6j9
Asaph speaks of “regions” as if they were containers in which one could put “places of violence.” Alternate translation: “violent people do evil deeds in dark places in the land wherever they can” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ul6r
The word “dark” is probably a metaphor for a place where bad things happen or for the lands to which the Israelites had been sent in exile, but these words should be translated literally if possible. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 74:21
## b3w6
“Do not let wicked people defeat the oppressed and make them ashamed”

## l26m
These are people who are treated cruelly by powerful people.

## z7yj
The words “poor” and “oppressed” mean basically the same thing and emphasize that Yahweh saves many who need his help. See how these words are translated in [Psalms 35:10](../035/010.md). (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 74:22
## be6c
“show everyone that you are right”

## a6kn
“Pay attention to.” God has not forgotten how fools insult him, but he does not seem to be thinking about it. See how this is translated in verse [Psalms 2](./001.md).

# 74:23
## c7dc
The word “voice” is a metonym for the words people use when they speak. Alternate translation: “what your adversaries are saying” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## n9n5
The psalmist speaks of the words of those who defy God as if they were the loud sound of animals or non-living objects like water or the wind. Alternate translation: “and pay attention to the loud and meaningless words of those who continually defy you” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## cyg8
boldly oppose

# 75:4
## ix6p
The words “the arrogant” and “the wicked” are nominal adjectives that can be translated with nouns. The verb for the second phrase may be supplied from the first. Alternate translation: “I said to the arrogant people … and I said to the wicked people” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]] and [[figs-ellipsis]])

## wl2r
Possible meanings are (1) God is speaking or (2) Asaph is speaking.

## yz7y
The speaker is speaking to many wicked people, so these forms are plural. (See: [[figs-pronouns]])

## wg7k
Asaph speaks of wicked people as if they were animals with horns on their heads, stretching their necks and holding their heads high to frighten other animals. Alternate translation: “Do not be confident” or “Do not boast about how strong you are” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 75:5
## zai8
The speaker is speaking to many wicked people, so these forms are plural. (See: [[figs-pronouns]])

## dl75
Asaph speaks of wicked people as if they were animals with horns on their heads, stretching their necks and holding their heads as high as they can to frighten other animals. Alternate translation: “Be especially sure not to boast that you are greater than God” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## cfl3
Asaph speaks of wicked people who defy or challenge God as if they were animals with horns on their heads, stretching their necks and holding their heads high to frighten other animals. Alternate translation: “arrogantly” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 75:6
## eqm6
Asaph speaks of the one who lifts up as if he were the action of lifting up. He also speaks of God giving strength and honor to a person as if God were physically lifting that person up. Alternate translation: “The one who will lift you up will not be someone who comes from the east” or “The one who will make you strong and have people honor you will not be someone who comes from the east” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 75:7
## h39y
See [[PSA-h39y]]
## ii5h
The objects of the verbs can be stated clearly: “he brings some people down and he lifts other people up.” The words “brings down” and “raises up” are metaphors for God making people powerful and taking away their power. Alternate translation: “he makes one man king in place of another man” or “he takes away one man’s power and gives power to another man” (See: [[figs-explicit]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 75:8
## hs8p
When Yahweh punishes the people they will be like people who have drunk strong wine and become ill. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## w9az
The foam is a metaphor for the power of the wine to make people drunk, Alternate translation: “strong wine” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

dried leaves or ground seeds

pours it from a large container into the cups that the people will drink from

## x1zf
“drink every drop of it”

## vl5i
God says

## eip6
The horns of an animal are a metaphor for the power of a person. Alternate translation: “take away all power from” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## qr6b
The horns of an animal are a metaphor for the power of a person. This can be translated as a statement in active form. Alternate translation: “I will raise up the horns of the righteous” or “I will make the righteous powerful” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 77:5
## u2n5
If necessary, “days of old” and “times long past” can be combined. Alternate translation: “about things that happened a very long time ago” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 77:6
## i4r9
This is an idiom for remembering. Alternate translation: “I remembered” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## kh2e
Another possible meaning is “what was happening.”

# 77:7
## ie4q
These two phrases express feeling rejection from the Lord. (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## gat1
“do things that show that he is pleased with me”

# 77:8
## ydt2
See [[PSA-ydt2]]
## d9gm
Asaph speaks of Yahweh no longer acting faithfully to his covenant as if Yahweh’s covenant faithfulness had gone away. The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be translated with an adjective. Alternate translation: “Has he stopped being faithful to his covenant forever?” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 77:9
## q3fw
These are two expressions stating similar concept of God not showing compassion. (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## qup5
Asaph speaks of anger as if it were a person shutting a door to keep another person, compassion, from coming out, Alternate translation: “Had God stopped showing us compassion because he was angry with us” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-rquestion]])

# 77:10
## v28j
Possible meanings are (1) the psalmist was speaking to himself or (2) the psalmist was speaking to Yahweh.

## m81u
The “right hand” is a metonym for power, authority or strength. Alternate translation: “the Most High no longer uses his power to help us” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 77:11
## xb3t
See [[PSA-xb3t]]
## esp5
“remember” See how this is translated in [Psalms 74:2](../074/002.md). (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## ul2k
“the wonderful things you did long ago”

# 77:12
## br6v
These two phrases express the concept of meditating on what Yahweh has done. (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## vsp7
think deeply about

## ju2f
“think of what they mean”

# 77:13
## hk88
See [[PSA-hk88]]
## tr7z
This can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “no god compares to our great God.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 77:14
## vh9m
The abstract noun “strength” can be translated with an adjective. Alternate translation: “shown people from many people groups how strong you are” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 77:15
## nc87
“gave us, your people, victory … us who are the descendants”

## cz77
The abstract noun “victory” can be translated with an adjective. Alternate translation: “caused your people to be victorious” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## ani3
This refers to the entire nation of Israel.

# 77:16
## ms5u
Asaph speaks of the water as if it were a person who saw something that terrified him. (See: [[figs-personification]])

## a22h
“water” and “depth” refers to large bodies of water such as the sea or ocean. (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## yh2l
deepest waters

# 77:17
## cbk2
Asaph speaks of the clouds as if they were people pouring water out of containers. Alternate translation: “Much rain fell” or “It rained very hard” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## cil6
This is a metaphor that describes lightning as God’s arrows. Alternate translation: “the lightning you made flashed like arrows” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## x29a
Use your language’s word for what an arrow does after someone shoots it.

# 77:18
## ks1j
This is personification, referring to the thunder as the voice of God. Alternate translation: “voice, which was as loud as thunder” or “very loud voice” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## nr2p
This is an exaggeration to emphasize that the lightning lit up everything the writer could see. Alternate translation: “lightning lit up everything as far as you could see” (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

# 77:19
## mc1k
These two phrases have similar meanings and are combined for emphasis. (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## xj78
Asaph is speaking of Yahweh as if Yahweh was a person with feet. While this is personification, it should be translated literally, if possible. (See: [[figs-personification]])

## k4y3
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “no one saw your footprints” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 77:20
## iq66
This simile compares God’s people to a flock of animals. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## w73a
The phrase “by the hand of” here means “by the action of” or “through the action of.”

## m5wn
Here “hand” is synechdoche for the whole person. (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 78:intro
## grf1
See [[PSA-grf1]]
# 78:1
## w9rf
See [[PSA-w9rf]]
## n8s4
This may refer to a style of music. See how you translated this in [Psalms 32:1](../032/001.md).

## d6i3
The noun “teaching” can be translated with a verb. Alternate translation: “Hear what I teach” or “Hear me as I teach you” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## eqz7
The word “mouth” represents the person. Alternate translation: “my words” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 78:2
## h2db
The idiom “open my mouth” means to speak. Alternate translation: “speak in parables” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## z2j2
“tell.” See how “pours out” is translated in [Psalms 19:2](../019/002.md).

## wp1x
If your language has a word for sayings that are purposely difficult to understand, you might use it here.

## c2kx

# 78:18
## tv84

## x27j

# 78:19
## di5n
the Israelites spoke

## gpm7
This can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “We do not believe that God can really lay out a table for us in the wilderness!” or “God, prove to us that you can really lay out a table for us in the wilderness!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## ui27
The idiom “lay out a table” means to prepare a table for a meal. Here the word “table” is a metonym for the food on the table. Alternate translation: “provide us with food” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 78:20
## xnn4
much water came out quickly

## pe8w
The people are laughing at God to insult him with these questions. Alternate translation: “But we will not believe that he can give us bread also or provide meat for his people until we see him do it.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## crp3
food from plants or food from animals. Though this is a merism for all kinds of food, it is best to translate it literally if possible. (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 78:21
## fd1k
The writer speaks of Yahweh’s anger as if it were a fire, and of Yahweh acting in anger against Jacob as if that fire burned Jacob. Alternate translation: “his anger was like a fire that burned Jacob” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## wp71
This refers to the nation of Israel

## k1pp
The writer speaks of Yahweh punishing Israel when he was angry as if his anger were a person who attacked Israel. Alternate translation: “because he was angry, he attacked Israel” (See: [[figs-personification]])

# 78:22
## ze3v
“did not trust him to save them”

# 78:23
## er7j
Asaph speaks of the skies as if they were a person who could hear and obey God’s commands. Alternate translation: “he spoke to the sky” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## bs7u
Possible meanings are (1) “sky” or (2) “clouds.”

## wx6j
Asaph speaks of the sky as if it were a storeroom with doors. Alternate translation: “opened the sky as if it were a storeroom” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 78:24
## mrr5
These two lines speak of the same event.

## u9dw
“He caused manna to fall from the sky like rain”

# 78:25
## l158
This refers to the manna that God provided for the people. The word “bread” represents food in general. Alternate translation: “the same kind of food that angels eat” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## a5x1
The abstract noun “abundance” can be translated with an adjective. Alternate translation: “abundant food” or “a large amount of food” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 78:26
## t8wt
“God caused”

# 78:27
## f33h
The writer speaks of Yahweh causing birds to fall from the sky as if the birds were rain that Yahweh caused to fall. He compares the large amount of birds to dust. Alternate translation: “He caused meat to fall from the sky like rain, and there was so much of it that it covered the ground like dust” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-simile]])

## x2ac
birds

## mw2h
No one can count the grains of sand. There were more birds than anyone could count, but this is an exaggeration: there were probably not literally the same number of birds as grains of sand. (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

# 78:29
## uu98
strongly desired

# 78:31
## sne1
while the food was still in their mouths ([Psalms 78:31](../078/031.md))

## pz58
“God was angry and attacked them.” See how “his anger attacked Israel” is translated in [Psalms 78:21](../078/021.md). (See: [[figs-personification]])

## qn52
This is a euphemism that means he caused them to die. Alternate translation: “killed” (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

# 78:32
## lg44
The word “deeds” is a metonym for God, who did the deeds. What they did not believe can be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “did not believe that he would take care of them even though he had done such wonderful deeds” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-explicit]])

# 78:33
## wg5l
See [[PSA-wg5l]]
## k4p9
Here the word “days” refers to a lifetime. The idiom “to cut short … days” means to cause someone to die before they have lived a long life. Alternate translation: “killed them while they were still young” (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

## b6nk
Asaph speaks of years as if they were containers. Alternate translation: “year after year they were afraid all the time” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 78:34
## njc9
to ask him what they needed to do so he would protect them (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## y7s9
These are different people from those whose days God “cut short.” When some of them died, others would “start to seek” God.

## kt48
“would repent” or “would be truly sorry for their sins”

## ut52
“look urgently” or “look as best they could”

# 78:35
## nd5q
See [[PSA-nd5q]]
## g7l1
“remember.” See how this is translated in [Psalms 20:3](../020/003.md).

## lz71
The writer speaks about God as if he were a hill or mountain where people could go to be safe from their enemies. Alternate translation: “God was the one who protected them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## xc3l
“the one who rescued them”

# 78:36
## bh83
“tell him he was wonderful when they did not believe it”

## u1ms
The word “mouth” is a metonym for the words they spoke using their mouths. Alternate translation: “by saying what they said” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 78:37
## dml1
Here “hearts” is a metonym for their thoughts. To be loyal to him is spoken of as being solidly attached to him. Alternate translation: “their thoughts were not focused on him” or “they were not loyal to him” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 78:38
## zr2s
“forgave them even though they had done evil deeds”

## x9x1
Asaph speaks of God’s anger as if it were an angry person whom God held onto to keep him from attacking the one who had made him angry. Alternate translation: “did not punish them even though he was angry with them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## n79l
Asaph speaks of God’s wrath as if it were a sleeping person whom God allowed to sleep and did not awaken. Alternate translation: “did not allow himself to become fully angry with them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 78:39
## uw3m
“remembered.” See how “call to mind” is translated in [Psalms 20:3](../020/003.md). (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## kj81
The word “flesh,” which is weak and then dies, is a metonym for human weakness and death. Alternate translation: “the Israelites were weak and would someday die” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 78:40
## pfz7
“places where nothing grows”

# 78:41
## e1t4
They wanted God to prove that he could do what he said he would do before they would believe him. See how you translated this in [Psalms 78:18](../078/018.md).

## hig2

## m414
a city in Egypt (See: [[translate-names]])

# 78:44
## f9kn
See [[PSA-f9kn]]
# 78:45
## k7ba
so many flies that it looked like a cloud

## iz2f
The flies made the Egyptians almost as unhappy as they would have if they had eaten the Egyptians. (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-hyperbole]])

## bl66
“went everywhere in their land”

# 78:46
## ze3g
“allowed the grasshoppers to eat all their crops and allowed the locusts to eat everything they had worked hard to produce”

## wy2b
a plant-eating insect with long legs used for jumping

## w8fu
Asaph speaks of the crops as a gift that God gave to the grasshoppers. Alternate translation: “He allowed the grasshopper to eat their crops” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## n1cm
“he gave their labor to the locust.” Asaph speaks of the people’s labor as if it were a gift that God gave to the grasshoppers. The word “labor” is a metonym for the crops that their labor had produced. Alternate translation: “he allowed the locusts to eat the crops they had worked so hard to produce” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 78:47
## wa4x
See [[PSA-wa4x]]
## kzp7
a tree that gives fruit

# 78:48
## m24k
lightning that makes loud thunder

## w4nt
“He brought hail” or “He caused hail to fall”

# 78:49
## jlg2
Asaph speaks of God’s fierceness as if it were a person who could attack another person. Alternate translation: “He was angry with them, so he suddenly and fiercely attacked them” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## f8zm
“His fierce anger”

## qct3
“attacked them when they were not expecting anything to happen”

## eh6a
Asaph speaks of wrath, fury, and trouble as though they are people God can send to do his work for him. Alternate translation: “He was so angry that he wanted to harm the Egyptians, so he made trouble for them and brought them to disaster” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-simile]])

## p12j
anger that makes someone want to harm others

# 78:50
## wz8d
See [[PSA-wz8d]]
## fr3i
The psalmist speaks of anger as if it were a person who could walk Yahweh getting ready to punish the people as if he were making a smooth road for a person to walk on. Alternate translation: “He was so angry that he did everything he could to harm them” or “It was as if his anger was an army and he made a smooth road for it to march on” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-personification]])

## p57t
“he did not keep the Egyptians from dying” or “he did not permit the Egyptians to live”

## d3e4
Asaph speaks of the plague as if it were a person who was going to harm the Egyptians. Alternate translation: “he made them all very ill with the plague” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-personification]])

# 78:51
## kng7
This phrase refers to the firstborn males of each family. Alternate translation: “the firstborn males” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## a3cw
Here the word “tents” is a metonym for families. The word “Ham” refers to Egypt by the name of their ancestor. Alternate translation: “among the families of Egypt” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 78:52
## s28c
See [[PSA-s28c]]
## bep8
The writer speaks about the Israelites as if they were sheep. This means God cared for and protected the people like a shepherd does his sheep. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 78:53
## fxw6
completely covered

# 78:54
## w66a
See [[PSA-w66a]]
## bzp1
The words “right hand” are a metonym for power. Alternate translation: “he won for himself using his own power” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 78:55
## er9f
Possible meanings are (1) God assigned the Israelites their inheritance in the land in which the other nations had once lived or (2) God assigned to the nations he had driven out an inheritance somewhere else. “gave them land that would always be theirs”

## ph3l
Possible meanings are (1) he settled Israel in their own tents in the land or (2) he settled Israel in the tents from which he had driven the other nations. Most of these “tents” were actually houses, both when the other nations lived in them and when the Israelites lived in them. Alternate translation: “in their homes” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 78:56
## ft4l
See [[PSA-ft4l]]
## be26
These words mean almost the same thing. The writer uses them both to emphasize that the Israelites did not believe that God would either provide for them or punish evil as he had said he would. (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## n34f
They wanted God to prove that he could do what he said he would do before they would believe him. See how you translated this idea in [Psalms 78:18](../078/018.md).

## wzz7
refused to obey

# 78:57
## dcj9
These words mean almost the same thing. The writer uses them both to emphasize that the Israelites did not do for God what they had said they would do. (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 78:58
## s4jj
See [[PSA-s4jj]]
## um4c
These two phrases are in parallel and have similar meanings. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 78:60
## g4ie
See [[PSA-g4ie]]
# 78:61
## l5jm
Asaph speaks of God’s strength and glory as if they were physical objects that people could capture and hold. The words “strength” and “glory” are probably mentonyms for the ark of the covenant. The word “hand” is a metonym for the enemy’s power. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “He allowed his enemies to capture the glorious ark of his covenant; he simply gave it to them so they could do whatever they wanted with it” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 78:62
## af4s
See [[PSA-af4s]]
## j359
Asaph speaks of God’s people as if they were a small object God would give as a gift, and of the sword, a metonym for death in war, as if it were a person who could receive a gift. Alternate translation: “He allowed people to kill all his people in war” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## tj9q
“he was angry with the people he had said would be his forever”

# 78:63
## zrz5
Possible meanings are (1) “The enemy used fire to kill all their young men” or (2) “Their young men died quickly in battle like a fire burns dry grass.” Do not communicate that these people used guns. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## fil5
To “devour” is to eat everything very quickly.

## l8nc
the celebration when people marry

# 78:64
## t7aj
Here the word “sword” represents soldiers who fought with swords. The phrase “fell by the sword” is an idiom that means to die in battle. Alternate translation: “Their priests died in battle” or “Enemies killed their priests with swords” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-idiom]])

## zgw6
Possible meanings are (1) someone forced the widows not to weep or (2) so many priests died that there was no time for proper funerals.

## cve4
women whose husbands have died

# 78:65
## p4ds
The Lord not acting for a period of time is spoken of as if he were asleep, and his beginning to act is spoken of as if he awakened. Alternate translation: “the Lord began to act as if he had awakened from sleep” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ny1d
Possible meanings are (1) like a warrior who had drunk too much wine and has become angry because he was awakened and so wants to fight or (2) like a warrior who drank much wine but is now able to think and fight well because he has slept.

# 78:67
## wl1g
See [[PSA-wl1g]]
## rr45
Here the word “tent” is a metonym for a family. In this phrase it refers to the descendants of Joseph. Alternate translation: “the descendants of Joseph” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## fzh7
Ephraim was Joseph’s son.

# 78:68
## as8e
Mount Zion was in the land where the tribe of Judah lived.

# 78:69
## dkr9
Possible meanings for this simile are (1) Yahweh has made his sanctuary as high as the heavens. Alternate translation: “He built his sanctuary high, like the heavens” or (2) Yahweh has made his sanctuary as permanent as the heavens. Alternate translation: “He built his sanctuary to last permanently, like the heavens last forever” (See: [[figs-simile]])

## bq5q
The verb may be supplied from the previous line. The writer compares the permanence of Yahweh’s sanctuary to the permanence of the earth. Alternate translation: “He built his sanctuary to last permanently, like the earth lasts permanently” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-simile]])

# 78:70
## w3bv
See [[PSA-w3bv]]
## c6qm
“from where he was working in the sheepfolds” [[figs-explicit]]

## c6y9
spaces with walls around them where sheep are kept safe

# 78:71
## x189
The word “shepherd” is a metaphor for one who leads and protects other people. Alternate translation: “to lead and protect the descendants of Jacob, his people, and of Israel, his heritage” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## f6sh
“the ones he had chosen to be his forever” See how this is translated in [Psalms 78:62](../078/062.md).

# 78:72
## q2pe
The word “shepherded” is a metaphor for leading and protecting. Alternate translation: “David led them and protected them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 79:intro
## k1lh
See [[PSA-k1lh]]
# 79:1
## q2wf
See [[PSA-q2wf]]
## yd8b
“the land that was to be yours forever” See how this is translated in [Psalms 68:9](../068/009.md).

# 79:3
## da6v
The word “blood” is a metonym for innocent life. To shed blood is to kill innocent people. Most people saw water every day, so for blood to be as common as water, many innocent people would have to die. Alternate translation: “They have killed so many innocent people that the blood is everywhere, like water after it rains” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-simile]])

# 79:4
## ab9w
The words “reproach,” “mocking,” and “derision” are metonyms for those whom others reproach, mock, and deride. Alternate translation: “We have become people whom our neighbors reproach; those around us mock and deride us” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## kc2x
The pronoun “We” refers to God’s people.

## beg9
strong laughter to shame a person

# 79:5
## us3y
This can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “It seems as though your jealous anger will never stop burning like fire.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## fx6v
The abstract noun “anger” can be stated as “angry.” Alternate translation: “will you be jealous and angry” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## g11j
This simile compares the expression of God’s anger to a fire that destroys things. Alternate translation: “destroy us” (See: [[figs-simile]] and [[figs-explicit]])

# 79:6
## dj2l
See [[PSA-dj2l]]
## ez9d
Asaph speaks of God’s wrath as if it were a liquid. Alternate translation: “Since you are angry, punish the nations” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## b6qd
The word “name” is a metonym for the person’s power and authority. Alternate translation: “do not belong to you” or “do not ask you to help them” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 79:7
## wy1b
The word “Jacob” is a mentonym for his descendants, the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “they have completely destroyed the people of Israel” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 79:8
## l3hg
“Do not continue to remember the sins of our forefathers and punish us for them” or “Forgive us for the sins of our forefathers”

## wp6e
The writer speaks of the people being weak and discouraged as if they were in a low position. Alternate translation: “we are very weak” or “we are very discouraged” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 79:9
## el14
The word “salvation” can be translated with the verb “save”: “God who saves us.” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## bk3k
The word “name” is a metonym for his reputation, for what people know about him. Alternate translation: “so that people will know about your glory” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## m8z9
God’s name here represents his whole being and the honor that he deserves. Alternate translation: “so that people will honor you” or “for your own sake” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 79:10
## pgc2
This can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “The nations should not be able to say, ‘Where is their God?’” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## q444
This taunt can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Their God cannot do anything!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## syh5
To shed blood is a metonym for killing innocent people. This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “Avenge your innocent servants whom the nations killed where we can see you do it” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-rquestion]])

## b56d
“in our sight” or “while we are present”

# 79:11
## ppy3
Asaph speaks of the sound made by prisoners in pain and sorrow as if it were a person who appears before a king. Alternate translation: “Listen carefully to the groans of the prisoners and help them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## ggp2
This is an idiom that refers to people who are condemned to death. Alternate translation: “those who are condemned to die” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 79:12
## mm85
Asaph speaks of the evil deeds that the neighboring countries did against Israel as “insults,” and as if they were physical items. He asks the Lord to count those deeds, and for every one that the neighboring countries committed, he asks the Lord to have someone do seven evil deeds to the neighboring countries. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## guy1
“Return” or “Give back”

## v85q
onto their knees and thighs as they are sitting. This is a metaphor for “directly and personally.” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 79:13
## uf6h
The word “sheep” is a metaphor for helpless people whom a shepherd protects and leads. Alternate translation: “we who are your people, whom you protect and lead, will thank you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## qt1i
“make sure that all generations to come know all the good things you have done”

## y9ux
This represents the things that people will praise them the Lord for. Alternate translation: “continue to praise you for the things that you have done” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 80:intro
## lis5
See [[PSA-lis5]]
# 80:1
## ijk8
See [[PSA-ijk8]]
## lsl8
“This is for the director of music to use in worship” See how this is translated in [Psalms 4:1](../004/001.md).

## gk4a
This may refer to a style of music. See how you translated this in [Psalms 45:1](../045/001.md).

## q2t5
Asaph is referring to God as the one who leads and protects Israel. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## aa1a
The word “Joseph” refers to the nation of Israel. Asaph speaks of the people as if they were a flock of sheep that Yahweh, who is the shepherd, leads. Alternate translation: “you who lead the descendants of Joseph as though they were a flock of sheep” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## zxu9
Here Joseph represents the nation of Israel. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## w585
You may need to make explicit that the cherubim are those on the lid of the ark of the covenant. The biblical writers often spoke of the ark of the covenant as if it were Yahweh’s footstool upon which he rested his feet as he sat on his throne in heaven above. Alternate translation: “you who sit on your throne above the cherubim on the ark of the covenant” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## qzy5
Asaph speaks of God as if he were the sun, giving light, a metaphor for moral goodness. Alternate translation: “give us light” or “show us the right way to live” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 80:2
## k49s
The phrase “stir up” means to “put into action.” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## h5vg
This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “and please save us” or “so that you can save us” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 80:8
## wf8l

The psalmist speaks of his people as if they were a plant that Yahweh was transplanting. Alternate translation: “you drove out nations from their land and gave it to us, the vine, and planted us there” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 80:10
## fi6a
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Its shade covered the mountains, its branches the cedars of God” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## bmv4
“and the cedars of God were covered by its branches,” which copies the verb from the previous sentence. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “and its branches covered the cedars of God” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## rx8y
Possible meanings are (1) “the highest cedar trees,” the cedar trees that grew on the “mountains” in the land of Lebanon north of Israel, or (2) “God’s own cedar trees.”

## xb2b

## x4p7
Possible meanings are (1) these are other spiritual beings who dwell in heaven. Alternate translation: “the divine beings” or “the heavenly judges” or (2) these are human judges that God has appointed. Either way, it does not mean they are gods like Yahweh is god. It means God has given them great power and authority. Alternate translation: “the rulers”

## h4lp
Yahweh uses a question to rebuke the gods for not judging people fairly. (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## c2bz

## ar42

## r4ui
The gods corrupting the moral order that Yahweh established is spoken of as if the gods were shaking the earth and making it fall apart. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## l1x8
fall apart

# 82:6
## ie8b
Here “gods” refers to the same group as in [Psalms 82:1](../082/001.md). Whether this refers to spiritual beings or human beings, they are not gods like Yahweh is God, and they are not literally his sons. By calling them “gods” and “sons of the Most High,” Yahweh is acknowledging that he has given them great power and authority.

## nch3
Yahweh is speaking about himself as “the Most High.” (See: [[figs-123person]])

## j3wm
Here “nations” represents the people of the nations. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## bm36

# 85:3
## n2va
God no longer punishing the Israelites is spoken of as if wrath were an object that Yahweh could take away from the Israelites. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## eh2w

# 85:4
## r347
