## u4ty
Asking God to stop being angry with the Israelites is spoken of as if displeasure were an object the writer wants God to let go of. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 85:5
## b6ih
The writer uses these questions to emphasize that he is requesting God to stop being angry with them. These rhetorical questions can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Please do not stay angry at us forever.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 85:6
## fc4x
The writer uses a question to emphasize his request to God to make the people of Israel prosper and happy again. This rhetorical question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Please make us prosper again.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 85:7
## am5x
If the sentence is rearranged, the ideas in the abstract noun “covenant faithfulness” can be expressed with the phrase “faithful to your covenant.” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## u94q
This is how the writer wants God to show his faithfulness to his people. If the sentence is rearranged, the ideas in the abstract noun “salvation” can be expressed with the verb “save.” Alternate translation: “and save us” or “by saving us” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 85:8
## f1q2
“have a peaceful relationship with his people” or “bring peace to his people”

## mt5f
A person changing the way he behaves is spoken of as if he were physically turning to a different direction. Alternate translation: “Yet they must not start doing foolish things again” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 85:9
## q85w
God being ready to save someone is spoken of as if salvation were an object that God has placed near someone. Alternate translation: “Surely God is ready to save those” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## w9na
Here “glory” represents God’s presence. Alternate translation: “then his glorious presence will remain in our land” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 85:10
## r9mp
The abstract nouns “faithfulness” and “trustworthiness” are spoken of as if they were persons who meet together. Possible meanings are (1) God has been faithful because of his covenant and has done what he promised to do. Alternate translation: “God has been faithful to his covenant and has proven that he is worthy of people trusting him” or (2) God has been faithful because of his covenant and the people have responded by being faithful to him. Alternate translation: “God has been faithful to his covenant and people have responded by trusting him” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-personification]])

## vxd4
Most likely the speaker is describing a time in the future when God causes the people to prosper again. Alternate translation: “will meet together … will kiss each other”

## ilq2
Possible meanings are (1) the people will do what is right and God will cause the people to live in peace or (2) God will do what is right and will cause the people to live in peace. Either way righteousness and peace are abstract nouns and are spoken of as if they were persons who kiss each other. (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-personification]])

## pbn4
This was a common way for friends to greet each other.

# 85:11
## y3gv
The people on earth being faithful to God is spoken of as if trustworthiness were a plant growing out of the ground. The abstract noun “trustworthiness” can be stated as “loyal.” Alternate translation: “Here on earth, we will be loyal to God” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## uw31
The word “righteousness” is an abstract noun, and it is spoken of as a person looking down as God does. Alternate translation: “God will look on us from heaven and will act justly towards us” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-personification]])

# 85:13
## bxa8
God doing what is right everywhere he goes is spoken of as if righteousness were a person who goes ahead of God and prepares a path for God to walk. (See: [[figs-personification]])

## b458
Here “footsteps” represents where God walks. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 86:intro
## yms3
See [[PSA-yms3]]
# 86:1
## ss24
See [[PSA-ss24]]
## j7gt
“weak and needy”

# 86:2
## b1gx
David refers to himself as “your servant,” that is, God’s servant. Alternate translation: “save me, your servant” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 86:3
## xel4
This is an exaggeration. David cries out continually, but not literally at all times from dawn to dusk. Alternate translation: “continually” (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

# 86:4
## fc6p
David refers to himself as “your servant,” that is, God’s servant. Alternate translation: “Make me, your servant” or “Make me” (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 86:7
## mlm4
“During my times of difficulty”

## xp82
“I pray to you”

# 86:8
## ey46
The writer is not acknowledging these other gods exist. He is referring to false gods that people from other nations worship. (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 86:9
## nvl8
Here “the nations” represent the people. Alternate translation: “The people from all nations” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## hb9u
Here “name” represents the whole person. Alternate translation: “They will honor you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 86:10
## ide9
“very good things that amaze me”

## qsw4
“you are the only God”

# 86:11
## sa8a
A person who obeys what God wants is spoken of as if he were walking on God’s way or road. Alternate translation: “Teach me your truth, Yahweh. Then I will obey what you say” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## up7s
Here “heart” represents a person’s thoughts, emotions, and motives. Asking God to cause a person to respect him completely is spoken of as if the person’s heart were in many pieces and that God unites them together. Alternate translation: “Cause me to respect you sincerely with all my heart” or “Cause me to respect you sincerely” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 86:12
## ln3t
Here “heart” represents a person’s thoughts, emotions, and motives. Alternate translation: “I will praise you completely and sincerely” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## r58p
Here “name” represents the whole person. Alternate translation: “I will glorify you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 86:13
## pa7h
The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be stated as an adjective. Alternate translation: “For you are very faithful to me because of your covenant” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## m9g3
This does not mean God brought him back to life after he died. It means God saved him when he was about to die.

# 86:14
## dx52
This nominal adjective can be stated as an adjective. Alternate translation: “arrogant people” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

## ih3b
“are coming together to harm me”

## tb73
This is a way of saying they want to kill him. (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 86:15
## l91z
God constantly being faithful and trustworthy is spoken of as if faithfulness and trustworthiness were objects of which God has a large quantity. The two abstract nouns can be translated as adjectives. Alternate translation: “always faithful to your covenant and very worthy of people trusting you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 86:16
## rlz9
Asking God to consider him is spoken of as if he wanted God to turn and look at him. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ha4f
The abstract noun “strength” can be stated as a verb or an adjective. Alternate translation: “strengthen your servant” or “make your servant strong” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## r4fq
The writer is speaking about himself in the third person. (See: [[figs-123person]])

# 86:17
## ja63
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “they will be ashamed” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 87:intro
## l8z3
See [[PSA-l8z3]]
# 87:1
## pr6p
See [[PSA-pr6p]]
# 87:2
## mz5w
Here “gates of Zion” represents the entire city of Jerusalem. Alternate translation: “city of Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## tah3
The people lived in tents while they wandered in the desert. Here the writer uses “tents of Jacob” to represent where the Israelites live now. Alternate translation: “any of the other dwelling places of the Israelites” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 87:3
## hrr7
The writer speaks to the city of Jerusalem as if it were listening to him. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “You people in Jerusalem, other people say wonderful things about your city” (See: [[figs-apostrophe]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 87:4
## mkk9
“I tell about.” Here “I” refers to Yahweh.

## x63j
Here “Rahab” is a poetic way of referring to Egypt. Both “Rahab” and “Babylon” represent the people. Alternate translation: “the people of Egypt and Babylon” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[translate-names]])

## x7tc
“to those who worship me”

## qzf2
“This one” refers to the people from the nations that the writer mentions. Although they were not physically born in Zion, those who follow God are natives of Jerusalem spiritually. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 87:5
## uer3
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “People will say about Zion” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## bqs5
People from other nations that worship Yahweh are spoken of as if they were born in Jerusalem. Alternate translation: “It is as though all of these people were born in Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## qxn4
It was common to refer to cities as “her.” Alternate translation: “in Jerusalem” or “in Zion”

## zdf6
The writer uses the reflexive pronoun “himself” to emphasize that it is the Most High who is doing this. (See: [[figs-rpronouns]])

## mx5t
“will make Jerusalem strong”

# 87:6
## p3jr
Yahweh acknowledging that the people from other nations belong to him is spoken of as if he were a king writing down the names of the people who live in his city. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## wgv4
“This one” refers to the people from the nations that the writer mentions. Although they were not physically born in Zion, those who follow God are natives of Jerusalem spiritually. See how you translated this in [Psalms 87:4](../087/004.md). (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 87:7
## q7a8
Jerusalem being a place where people receive all their blessings is spoken of as if Jerusalem were a spring that provided water to the people. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 88:intro
## w2mk
See [[PSA-w2mk]]
# 88:1
## vi6i
See [[PSA-vi6i]]
## d8eg
“This is a psalm that the sons of Korah wrote”

## qm8l
“this is for the director of music to use in worship”

## f9te
This may refer to a style of music. (See: [[translate-transliterate]])

## i9mw
This may refer to a style of music. See how you translated this in [Psalms 32:1](../032/001.md).

## l37h
This is the name of a man. (See: [[translate-names]])

## w1cg
The abstract noun “salvation” can be stated as a verb. Alternate translation: “you are the one who saves me” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## hj2g
The writer uses these words with opposite meanings to say that he cries out continually. (See: [[figs-merism]])

# 88:3
## j9c1
The writer speaks of himself as if he were a container and troubles are the contents that fill the container. Alternate translation: “For I am very troubled” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## tee7
Here “life” represents the writer. And “Sheol” represents death. The writer speaks about himself possibly dying soon as if Sheol were a place and he has arrived at that place. Alternate translation: “I am about to die” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 88:4
## xl1m
The word “pit” means the same as “Sheol.” The phrase “go down into the pit” represents dying. Alternate translation: “people treat me like I have already died” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 88:5
## yvx8
People treating the writer as if he were already dead is spoken of as if he were a corpse that they left unburied. Alternate translation: I am left alone as if I were dead” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## yw6f
The writer feeling like people and God have abandoned him speaks about himself as if he were already a dead person lying in a grave. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## hf3g
The nominal adjective “the dead” can be stated as an adjective. Alternate translation: “a dead person who lies” or “dead people who lie” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

## khp3
“who no longer receive your care” or “people you have stopped caring about”

## wf13
God no longer using his power to help dead people is spoken of as if God literally cut him off or removed him from his power. Alternate translation: “you no longer use your power to help them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 88:6
## z7j5
The writer feeling like God has abandoned him speaks about himself as if God has put the him in the deepest and darkest grave. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 88:7
## b6u3
This speaks about God being very angry with the writer as if God’s wrath were a heavy object lying on top of the writer. Alternate translation: “I feel your great anger” or “I feel how very angry you are with me” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## te8c
This speaks about God being very angry with the writer as if God’s anger were large waves rising up from the sea then landing on the writer. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 88:8
## vss9
“those who know me”

## vb5j
“You have made me a disgusting sight to them” or “Because of you, they are shocked when they see me”

## ezj9
The writer’s physical condition that makes him repulsive to his friends is spoken of as if he were in closed into a confining space. Alternate translation: “It is as though I were in a prison” or “I am trapped” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 88:9
## k5hb
Here “eyes” represents a person’s ability to see. For his eyes to grow weary from trouble is a way of saying that he his troubles cause him to cry so much that it is hard for him to see. (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-idiom]])

## af1k
“I lift up my hands to you.” This is an action that shows that he totally depends on God. (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 88:10
## q775
The writer uses a question to emphasize that if God lets him die then God will no longer be able to do wonderful things for him. Alternate translation: “You do not do wonders for dead people.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## nv8l
The writer uses a question to emphasize that if God lets him die then he will not be able to praise God any longer. Alternate translation: “You know that those who have died will not stand up and praise you.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 88:11
## a7rt
Both questions mean the same thing. The writer uses questions to emphasize that a dead person is not able to praise God’s faithfulness. The abstract nouns “faithfulness” and “loyalty” can be translated as adjectives. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Nobody will proclaim your covenant faithfulness or loyalty from the grave.” or “Nobody will proclaim from the grave that you are faithful to your covenant and loyal to your people” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## pt2z
These represent the place where people go after they die.

## vbv3
This can be translated as a separate sentence. Alternate translation: “Will your loyalty be proclaimed in the place of the dead?” or “Those who are dead will not proclaim your loyalty.” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-rquestion]])

# 88:12
## ec9z
These represent the place where people go after they die.

## lf46
Both questions mean the same thing. The writer uses questions to emphasize that those who are dead are not able to experience or proclaim the great things God does. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “People will not talk about your wonderful deeds and righteousness in the dark place of the forgotten dead.” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## u1h9
This can be translated as a separate sentence. Alternate translation: “Will your righteousness be known in the place of forgetfulness?” or “Those who are in the place of forgetfulness will not know about the righteous things you do.” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-rquestion]])

# 88:13
## tle5
The writer praying to Yahweh is spoken of as if the prayer itself goes to speak with Yahweh. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 88:14
## wg44
This speaks of the writer feeling like God has rejected or abandoned him as if God were hiding his face or physically turning away from the writer. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 88:16
## q3hi
This speaks of God punishing the writer as if God’s actions are a large wave rising out of the sea that fall on and crush the writer. Alternate translation: “It is as if your angry actions crush me” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## e9zd
This exaggeration speaks about God punishing the writer as if God has completely destroyed the writer. Alternate translation: “the terrifying things you do have destroyed me” or “the terrifying things you do have almost destroyed me” (See: [[figs-hyperbole]])

# 88:17
## z4t8
The writer compares God’s “angry actions” and “terrifying deeds” to a flood of water. Alternate translation: “All day long they threaten to destroy me like a flood” (See: [[figs-simile]])

## e9s9
The word “They” refers to God’s “angry actions” and “terrifying deeds” from the previous verse.

## qva9
The writer speaks of God’s “angry actions” and “terrifying deeds” as if they were enemies who were trying to capture and kill him. Alternate translation: “they have surrounded me like enemy soldiers” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 88:18
## x6gd
“every person I love and know”

## pnc1
This speaks about darkness as if it were a person that could be friends with someone else. The writer is emphasizing that he feels completely alone. Alternate translation: “Everywhere I go it is dark” (See: [[figs-personification]])

# 89:intro
## aw17
See [[PSA-aw17]]
# 89:1
## knd1
See [[PSA-knd1]]
## mu2k
This may refer to a style of music. See how you translated this in [Psalms 32:1](../032/001.md).

## dqe7
This is the name of the writer. (See: [[translate-names]])

## sv5i
The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be stated as an adjective. Alternate translation: “faithful acts” or “loving acts” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 89:2
## pbm7
God always doing what he promised to do is spoken of as if his faithfulness were a building that God has built and made firm. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “You will always be faithful because of your covenant with us” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## hn1i
God always doing what he promised to do is spoken of as if his faithfulness were a building that God has built and made firm. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## l9ik
Possible meanings are (1) this refers to God’s dwelling place. This would mean God rules from heaven and he always does what he promises or (2) this refers to the sky. This would mean that God’s promises are as constant and permanent as the sky .

# 89:3
## d13j
You can make explicit that “chosen one” refers to David. Alternate translation: “I made a promise to David, the one I chose” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## y54c
Yahweh is speaking in 89:3-4.

# 89:4
## yr1g
Yahweh always causing one of David’s descendants to be king is spoken of as if David’s descendants are a building that Yahweh will build and make firm. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## p2z4
Here “throne” represents the power to rule as king. The psalmist speaks of God promising that one of David’s descendants will always rule as king as if God will build David’s throne and make it firm. Alternate translation: “I will make sure that one of your descendants will rule as king over every generation of my people” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 89:5
## m81y
Here “heavens” represents those who are in heaven. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## e553
“praise you, Yahweh, because of the wonderful things you do”

## gs3g
The abstract noun “truthfulness” can be stated as “you always do what you promise to do.” This can also be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “The assembly of the holy ones praises you because you always do what you promise to do” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## g9qz
This refers to the angels in heaven.

# 89:6
## c358
Both questions mean the same thing. The writer is using question to emphasize that there in no one in heaven like Yahweh. (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-rquestion]])

## vj1k
Here “sons of” means having the characteristics of. This is a way of referring to other spiritual beings that live in heaven. Alternate translation: “angels” or “divine beings” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 89:7
## rgy8
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “The council of the holy ones greatly honors God” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## vtz8
“the gathering of heavenly beings” or “the gathering of angels”

# 89:8
## cs88
The writer asks the question to emphasize there is no one as strong as Yahweh. (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## niz2
Yahweh always dong what he promises to do is spoken of as if his truthfulness were a cloak or garment that wraps around him. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 89:9
## t34l
“You control the raging sea”

# 89:10
## q99x
The word “Rahab” here refers to a monster of the sea. (See: [[figs-explicit]] and [[translate-names]])

## uhp9
Here “arm” represents power. Alternate translation: “with your great power” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:12
## di8b
The writer uses the words “north” and “south” together to mean that God created everything everywhere. (See: [[figs-merism]])

## y2hj
Tabor is a mountain southwest of the sea of Galilee and Hermon is a mountain northeast of the sea of Galilee. The writer describes these mountains as if they were persons who could rejoice. Alternate translation: “It is as if Mount Tabor and Mount Hermon were rejoicing in your name” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[translate-names]])

## b76n
Here “name” represents the whole person. Alternate translation: “in you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:13
## j9ue
The words “mighty arm,” “strong hand,” and “right hand” all represent God’s power. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## ldw5
To raise the right hand high into the air is a gesture that indicates power. (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 89:14
## hyu5
God ruling as king and doing what is right and just is spoken of as if God’s throne were a building, and righteousness and justice were its foundation. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## w4d5
The throne represents God’s rule as king. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## x3in
God always being faithful and doing what he promises to do is spoken of as if covenant faithfulness and trustworthiness come and meet with God. The abstract nouns can be translated as adjectives. Alternate translation: “You are always faithful to your covenant and are worthy of people trusting you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 89:15
## a8ms
Here the word “worship” implies shouting and blowing horns. These were common acts of worship during Israelite festivals. (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## p2gi
Here people living their lives is spoken of as if they were walking. Alternate translation: “they live” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## v1rr
The writer speaks of Yahweh acting favorably towards them as if Yahweh’s face shone a light on them. Alternate translation: “knowing that you act favorably towards them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 89:16
## j2hd
Here “name” represents the person. Alternate translation: “in you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## uz6z
The abstract noun “righteousness” can be stated as an adjective. Alternate translation: “they exalt you because you always do what is right” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 89:17
## f1h8
The abstract noun “strength” can be stated as an adjective. Alternate translation: “You make them wonderfully strong” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## w9hu
Here “their” refers to the Israelites. The writer would have included himself as a member of the Israelites. Alternate translation: “You are our” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## t45a
Here “we” refers to the writer and the Israelites but not to Yahweh, to whom he speaks. (See: [[figs-exclusive]])

# 89:18
## nu6c
The king who protects his people and whom Yahweh chose is spoken of as if he were a shield that belongs to Yahweh. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 89:19
## e2wr
See [[PSA-e2wr]]
## erw7
To place a crown on a person’s head is a sign of making him king. Alternate translation: “I have made a mighty man king” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## fxq4
Some Bible translations have “given strength to a mighty one” or “helped a mighty one.”

## ss27
Here “raised up” means appointed. It is implied that God chose this person to be king. Alternate translation: “I have chosen one from among the people to be king” (See: [[figs-idiom]] and [[figs-explicit]])

# 89:20
## nkd2
Here to pour oil on someone’s head is a sign that God is appointing the person to be king. (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 89:21
## u6uc
Here “hand” and “arm” both means the power and control of Yahweh. Alternate translation: “I will support him and make him strong” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-parallelism]])

# 89:22
## h3tn
The writer refers to those who have the nature or character of wickedness as “sons of wickedness.” Alternate translation: “wicked person” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:24
## x8f4
See [[PSA-x8f4]]
## v3cj
God always being loyal and doing what he promises for David is spoken of as if truth and faithfulness were objects that would be with David. The abstract nouns “truth” and “faithfulness” can be translated as adjectives. Alternate translation: “I will always be truthful with him and I will always act faithfully towards him” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## q652
Here “name” represents God’ power. Alternate translation: “I, God, will cause him to be victorious” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:25
## k7aa
Here “hand” and “right hand” represent power and authority. Here “the sea” seems to refer to the Mediterranean Sea west of Israel, and “rivers” refers to the Euphrates river in the east. This means David will have authority over everything from the sea to the river. Alternate translation: “I will give him authority over everything from the Mediterranean sea to the Euphrates river” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-merism]])

# 89:26
## hc3j
This has a quotation within a quotation. It can be stated as an indirect quotation. Alternate translation: “He will say that I am his Father, his God, and the rock of his salvation.” (See: [[figs-quotesinquotes]] and [[figs-quotations]])

## ue8t
Yahweh protecting and saving David is spoken of as if Yahweh were a high rock that David could stand on top of for protection from enemies. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 89:27
## m5u8
See [[PSA-m5u8]]
## i4nj
Yahweh giving David special rank and privileges over all other people is spoken of as if David would be Yahweh’s firstborn son. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 89:28
## zs6u
Yahweh speaks of continuing to act faithfully towards David is spoken of as if Yahweh’s covenant faithfulness were an object that he extends or makes longer. The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be translated with an adverb. Alternate translation: “I will continue to act faithfully towards him forever” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 89:29
## nlx7
Someone from David’s family always ruling as king is spoken of as if his throne would last as long as the sky will last. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## k6mi
Here “throne” represents the power to rule as king. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:30
## zb7x
“David’s descendants”

# 89:32
## s8vu
God punishing David’s descendants is spoken of as if he would hit them with a rod. The abstract noun “rebellion” can be stated as a verb. Alternate translation: “punish them for rebelling against me” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## fq8i
God punishing David’s descendants is spoken of as if God would literally hit them. This can be stated as a complete sentence. Alternate translation: “I will punish them because they sinned against me” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-ellipsis]])

# 89:33
## k75f
This can be stated in positive form. Alternate translation: “I will always love David, and I will do what I promised to him” (See: [[figs-litotes]])

# 89:34
## m6m3
Here “lips” represent the whole mouth and the person speaking. Alternate translation: “what I said” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 89:36
## by6y
The words “will continue” are understood. Alternate translation: “his throne will continue as long as the sun before me” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## tm79
Here “throne” represents the power to rule as king. God is promising that one of David’s descendants will always be king. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## w6yh
God compares David’s rule as king to the sun to emphasize that someone from David’s descendants will always rule as king. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 89:37
## mwr5
Here “It” stands for David’s throne or power to rule as king. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “I will cause it to last forever” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## je44
God compares David’s rule as king to the moon to emphasize that someone from David’s descendants will always rule as king. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## gb6v
The moon is spoken of as if it were a person who witnesses God making this promise to David. Alternate translation: “the moon, which is like a faithful witness in the sky” (See: [[figs-personification]])

# 89:38
## exj7
The words “the king” are understood. Alternate translation: “you have refused and rejected the king” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## qnj2
“with the king you chose”

# 89:39
## c1wc
“You have rejected the covenant”

## prp1
To push down into the ground or dust is a sign of great humiliation. Alternate translation: “You have defiled his crown on the ground” or “You have caused his crown to fall into the dirt” (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## tbi4
This represents the king’s power as king and his right to rule. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:40
## lz6r
This implies that God allowed enemies to destroy the defenses at Jerusalem. Alternate translation: “You have allowed enemies to break down his walls and ruin his strongholds in Jerusalem” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 89:41
## ed5r
Here “neighbors” means people of nearby nations.

# 89:42
## i7mp
Here “right hand” represents power. To “raise the right hand” means that Yahweh has made his enemies strong enough to defeat God’s chosen king. (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[translate-symaction]])

# 89:43
## g8dt
Here “sword” represents the king’s power in battle. To turn the sword back represents making the king unable to win in battle. (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-idiom]])

## c3fd
Here “edge” represents the whole sword. Alternate translation: “his sword” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## w77q
Here “stand” represents being victorious in battle. Alternate translation: “You have not helped him to be victorious in battle” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:44
## g7kf
Here “throne” represents the power to rule as king. Alternate translation: “you, Yahweh, have ended his reign as king” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:45
## hg97
This is a way of saying God made the king seem old even while he was still young. Alternate translation: “Even while he is young you have made him weak like an old man” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## b231
Yahweh completely humiliating the king is spoken of as if shame were a garment that God used to cover the king. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 89:46
## r3l1
The writer uses these questions to emphasize that he does not want God to continue to refuse the king. Alternate translation: “Please, Yahweh, do not refuse to help the king forever.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## mu5e
God not helping the king is spoken of as if God were hiding from him. Alternate translation: “Will you refuse to help the king” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## q873
The writer uses a question to emphasize that he does not want God to remain angry. Alternate translation: “Please do not continue being angry” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## y4fv
God being very angry is spoken of as if his anger were a hot fire. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 89:47
## ajl2
The abstract noun “uselessness” can be stated as “uselessly.” Alternate translation: “that you have created all people to die uselessly” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## fe7m
This refers to humans in general. Alternate translation: “humans” or “people” (See: [[figs-gendernotations]])

# 89:48
## er51
The writer uses these questions to emphasize that all people will die. Alternate translation: “No one can live forever or bring himself back to life after dying” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## gg9s
Here “hand” refers to power. The writer speaks of Sheol as if it were a person who has power over those who die. Possible meanings are (1) a person cannot bring himself back to life after dying or (2) a person cannot keep himself from dying. (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-personification]])

# 89:49
## hvz7
The writer uses a question to request the Lord to be faithful to his covenant with David. Alternate translation: “Lord, be faithful to your covenant with David as you have been in the past” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## b6dc
The abstract nouns “acts” and “faithfulness” can be translated with a verb and an adverb. Alternate translation: “the things that you did before that showed that you are faithful to your covenant” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 89:50
## v1jl
The writer asks Yahweh to bring this up in his memory. Alternate translation: “Remember” or “Consider” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## r6c3
Here “your servants” refers to the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “how they mock us, your servants”

## iq2x
Here “heart” represents the person’s emotions. Alternate translation: “I endure so many insults from the people of the nations” or “I suffer because the people from the nations insult me” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## rn7t
Here “nations” represents the people of the nations. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:51
## fjd4
The enemies shouting insults at the king is spoken of as if insults were an object that the enemies throw violently at the king. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## vq6j
Here “footsteps” represents where the king goes. Alternate translation: “they mock your anointed one wherever he goes” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 89:52
## hvn5
See [[PSA-hvn5]]
## fig1
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “May people praise Yahweh forever” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## h8fn
The word “Amen” is repeated to emphasize approval of what has been said. See how you translated this in [Psalms 41:13](../041/013.md).

# 90:intro
## gmu6
See [[PSA-gmu6]]
# 90:1
## jue7
See [[PSA-jue7]]
## n3vq
God protecting his people is spoken of as if God were a refuge or shelter. Alternate translation: “Lord, you have been like a shelter for us” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ykl6
“always”

# 90:2
## i1f4
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Before you formed the mountains” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## b7ju
“were created” or “were shaped”

## mz45
This represents everything that is in the world. (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## x21e
This phrase represents all time past, present, and future.

# 90:3
## spk9
This implies that just as God created the first man, Adam, from the soil, God will cause people’s bodies to turn back into soil after they die. Alternate translation: “You return people to dust when they die” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## yjk2
Here “man” means people in general. (See: [[figs-gendernotations]])

## j396
The words “to dust” or “to soil” are understood. Alternate translation: “Return to dust, you descendants” or “Return to soil, you descendants” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## ijh1
This is a way of referring to humans in general. Alternate translation: “you humans” or “you people” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

# 90:4
## zt2t
The writer means that a long period of time seems like a short period of time to God. Alternate translation: “You consider a thousand years the same as one day in the past, or as a few short hours in the night” or “Even a thousand years is not a long time to you” (See: [[figs-simile]])

## b3xf
“1,000 years” (See: [[translate-numbers]])

## qd6k
Here sight represents judgment or evaluation. Alternate translation: “to you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 90:5
## prp6
God causing people to die suddenly is spoken of as if he swept them away with a broom. This sweeping is also spoken of as if it were a flood that carried people away. Alternate translation: “Lord, you destroy the people as with a flood and they die” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-simile]])

## wws1
This is a polite way of speaking of people dying. (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

# 90:6
## k25r
Both of these phrases refer to how the grass grows. Alternate translation: “it begins to grow and develop” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## udh3
Both of these phrases refer to how the grass dies. Alternate translation: “it fades and dries up” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

# 90:7
## vhb5
God destroying people in his anger is spoken of as if God’s anger were a fire that completely burns up the people. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “You destroy us in your anger” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

## qp5h
“when you are angry we are greatly afraid”

# 90:8
## fbk1
God considering people’s sins is spoken of as if sins were an objects he could place in front of himself and look at them. Alternate translation: “You see every sinful thing we do, even the sinful things we do in secret” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 90:9
## tw9c
The word “because” can be used to make clear that God’s wrath causes human life to end. Alternate translation: “Our lives come to an end because of your wrath” (See: [[grammar-connect-words-phrases]])

## z6eu
The writer compares human life to a sigh to emphasize that life is very brief. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 90:10
## bb7z
“70” (See: [[translate-numbers]])

## f12z
“80” (See: [[translate-numbers]])

## ydb3
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Even in our best years we experience trouble and sorrow” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## cun8
This is a polite way of referring to people dying. Alternate translation: “we die” (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

# 90:11
## xl7b
The writer uses a question to emphasize that no one has fully experienced God anger. Therefore no one truly reveres God and fears his anger when people sin. Alternate translation: “No one knows the intensity of your anger. Therefore no one fears your wrath when they sin.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 90:13
## lcn8
Asking Yahweh to no longer be angry is spoken of as if the writer wants God to turn physically away from his anger. Alternate translation: “Yahweh, please do not be angry any longer” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## e9p1
The writer uses a question to state that he wants God to stop being angry. (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## zs74
Here “your servants” refers to the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “Be merciful to us, your servants”

# 90:14
## p6kf
“Satisfy us every morning”

## k6eg
The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be stated as an adjective. Alternate translation: “by being faithful because of your covenant with us” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 90:15
## z79l
Both of these phrases mean the same thing. The writer is asking Yahweh to make them happy for the same amount of time he punished them. Alternate translation: “for the same amount of time that you afflicted us and caused us to suffer” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 90:16
## tb62
Here “your servants” refer to the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “Let us, your servants” or “Let us” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## g2qt
Here “children” means children and descendants.

## sjz5
The abstract noun “majesty” can be stated as “great things.” Alternate translation: “see the great things you do” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 90:17
## at2j
The abstract noun “favor” can be stated as “be kind.” Alternate translation: “May the Lord our God be kind to us” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## ye67
Here “hands” represents the whole person. Alternate translation: “cause us to be successful” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 91:intro
## ye5h
See [[PSA-ye5h]]
# 91:1
## xv6f
See [[PSA-xv6f]]
## g3bv
Because “live” and “stay” mean almost the same thing, as do “shelter” and “shadow,” which are both metaphors for protection, you may need to combine the two lines into one. Alternate translation: “The Most High, the Almighty, will care for all those who live where he can protect them” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-doublet]])

## lc6j
The word “shelter” is a metaphor for protection. Alternate translation: “who lives where the Most High protects him” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## jl84
The words “the Most High” refer to Yahweh. See how this is translated in [Psalms 18:13](../018/013.md).

## qi6d
The word “shadow” here is a metaphor for protection. Alternate translation: “will stay where the Almighty can protect him” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## jmc8
the one who has power and control over everything. See how this was translated in [Psalms 68:14](../068/014.md)

# 91:2
## kwv7
“I will say about Yahweh”

## d5cu
A “refuge” is any place a person can go and have someone or something protect him. A “fortress” is something that people make so they can protect themselves and their property. Asaph uses them here as metaphors for protection. Alternate translation: “the one to whom I can go and he will protect me” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 91:3
## ss8i
The words that have been left out can be put in. Alternate translation: “For God will rescue you from the snare of the hunter and he will rescue you from the plagues that can kill” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## laj3
“from the snare that the hunter has set to catch you”

# 91:4
## k4cn
God’s protection is here referred to as “wings” which a bird uses to cover its’ young from danger. “Cover you with his wings” and “under his wings” mean basically the same thing. Alternate translation: “He will keep you safe and protect you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-parallelism]])

## j3p2
God’s trustworthiness is here referred to as a “shield” which can protect people who rely on him. The abstract noun “trustworthiness” can be stated as “trust.” Alternate translation: “You can trust him to protect you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## f8d9
No one knows for sure what this word means. Possible meanings are (1) a small shield strapped to the forearm that soldiers used to defend themselves against arrows and swords or (2) a wall of stones set in a circle inside which soldiers could hide and shoot arrows.

# 91:5
## yp8k
The psalmist speaks of “terror” as if it were a spirit or fierce animal that could attack at night and so terrify people. Alternate translation: “things attacking you at night” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## g6en
These two phrases taken together refer to all possible times of night and day. (See: [[figs-merism]])

## b8kc
“Arrow” here is a metonym for the people who shoot the arrows. Alternate translation: “people attacking you with arrows during the day” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 91:6
## c6xg
The psalmist speaks of illness as if it were a person who went about at night killing other people. Alternate translation: “You will not be afraid of dying from illness” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## gba8
goes wherever he wants to go at any time

## y6eu
These two phrases taken together refer to all possible times of night and day. (See: [[figs-merism]])

## t9ab
an illness that makes many people ill at the same time

# 91:7
## c8n1
“Many people may fall all around you.” If translating the exact numbers confuses the readers, you may omit them.

## z1e3
“the evil will not harm you”

# 91:8
## ad8c
“You yourself will not suffer, but you will watch carefully, and you will see”

## dv9f
The abstract noun “punishment” can be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “how God punishes the wicked” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 91:9
## yz6c
The writer speaks of Yahweh protecting him as if Yahweh were a safe place for him to find protection from his enemies. Alternate translation: “Yahweh protects me, like a person finds protection in a place of refuge” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## k71v
“You should make the Most High your refuge also.” The psalmist stops speaking to God and speaks to the reader.

# 91:10
## ey4g
The writer speaks of a person experiencing bad or evil things as if evil were a person who overtakes another person. Alternate translation: “Nothing evil will happen to you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-personification]])

## emx9
The psalmist speaks of people who afflict others as though they were the harm they cause. Alternate translation: “no one will be able to harm your family” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 91:11
## pe5b
“Yahweh will command”

## gjx8
The psalmist speaks of the way a person lives his life as if it were a path down which the person walked. Alternate translation: “in everything you do” or “at all times” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 91:12
## v8h1
“Yahweh’s angels will lift you”

## dkd1
The angels will protect not only the reader’s foot but the rest of his body as well. The words “not … hit your foot on a stone” is an exaggerated metonym to say that not even small bad things will happen. This should probably not be taken as a promise that no bad things will ever happen again. Alternate translation: “you will not even hit your foot on a stone” or “not even the smallest bad thing will happen to you” (See: [[figs-hyperbole]] and [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 91:13
## bq3y
Where “hit your foot on a stone” is a minor problem, lions and snake are examples of great dangers. The psalmist speaks of lions and snakes as if they were small enough to be crushed under a person’s foot. Alternate translation: “You will be able kill lions and adders as if they were small animals you could crush under your feet” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## sw74
types of poisonous snake.

## qme2
crush by walking heavily on

# 91:14
## hzl3
“Because he loves me”

# 91:15
## cwn1
This can be made explicit. Alternate translation: “I will be with him when he is in trouble” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## am3h
The abstract noun “victory” can be translated with an adjective. Alternate translation: “I will cause him to be victorious” or “I will enable him to defeat his enemies” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 91:16
## jc9f
The idiom “the length of his days” here refers to the duration of his life. It may imply a long life. Alternate translation: “I will satisfy him by giving him a long life” or “I will make him happy by letting him live a very long time” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## xnz9
“and I will show him my salvation.” The psalmist speaks of the work God does to save people as if it were a physical object. Alternate translation: “I will save him so that he knows I am the one who saved him” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 92:intro
## cs72
See [[PSA-cs72]]
# 92:1
## bx9h
See [[PSA-bx9h]]
## fur9
The words “your name” are a metonym for “you.” Alternate translation: “to sing praises to you” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 92:2
## b75i
The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be translated with the adjective “faithful.” Alternate translation: “proclaim in the morning that you are faithful to your covenant” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## s7d2
The verb may be supplied from the previous phrase. The abstract noun “truthfulness” can be translated with the adjective “true.” Alternate translation: “proclaim every night that everything you say is true” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 92:4
## mn13
happy

## m1f8
The abstract noun phrase “your deeds” can be translated with the relative clause “what you have done.” Alternate translation: “by what you have done” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## if93
The abstract noun phrase “the deeds of your hands” can be translated with the relative clause “what your hands have done.” The word “hands” is a synecdoche for the whole person. Alternate translation: “What you have done” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-synecdoche]])

## ke7g
The abstract noun phrase “your deeds” can be translated with a relative clause. Alternate translation: “what you have done” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 92:5
## j4cz
“We cannot understand what you plan to do until you do it”

## syp5
The abstract noun phrase “your thoughts” can be translated with a relative clause. Alternate translation: “What you think” or “What you plan” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 92:6
## j8wu
animal-like

# 92:7
## a97q
This compares evil people to grass, which grows quickly and in many places. Alternate translation: “When evil people appear quickly and seem to be everywhere, like grass” (See: [[figs-simile]])

## w41d
This can be translated in active form. Alternate translation: “God has decided that he will destroy them completely” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 92:9
## cb78
Many texts do not have these words.

## u85w
“They will die” or “You will kill them”

## dk5v
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “you will scatter all those who do evil” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## l7k2
Many texts read, “have been scattered.”

## g3wa
“driven away”

# 92:10
## e26g
The psalmist speaks of God as if he had made him as strong as a wild animal. Here his “horn” represents his strength. Alternate translation: “You have made me as strong as a wild ox” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-simile]])

## gid3
Possible meanings are that the oil that God has put on the psalmist is a metaphor for God (1) making him happy “you have made me very happy” or (2) making him strong, “you have made me strong” or (3) enabling him to defeat his enemies, “you have enabled me to defeat my enemies.” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 92:11
## kpw5
The words “eyes” and “ears” are synecdoches for the person who sees and hears. The lines may be combined. Alternate translation: “I have seen and heard of the defeat of my evil enemies” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 92:12
## q7cc
Possible meanings are that righteous people will be like a healthy palm tree because they will (1) be strong or (2) live for long time. (See: [[figs-simile]])

## dyq1
Possible meanings are that righteous people will be like a healthy cedar tree growing in the land of Lebanon because (1) they will be strong or (2) people will honor them. (See: [[figs-simile]])

# 92:13
## lk33
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “Yahweh has planted them” or “Yahweh takes care of them as if they were trees he had planted” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## sh46
The psalmist speaks of people who truly worship God as if they were trees growing in Yahweh’s house. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## g7gv
The psalmist speaks of righteous people as if they were healthy trees. Alternate translation: “they are growing well” or “they are very strong” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## rg5j
in the courtyard of the temple in Jerusalem.

# 92:14
## pf43
The psalmist speaks of righteous people as if they were trees that produce food. Alternate translation: “They please God” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## m2x1
The psalmist speaks of righteous people as if they were trees. Alternate translation: “they remain strong and healthy” or “they always do what pleases God” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 92:15
## sev8
These words continue the thought begun by the words “they stay fresh and green” in ([Psalms 92:14](../092/014.md)). Possible meanings are (1) “they stay fresh and green, so that they can proclaim” or (2) “they stay fresh and green. This shows that.”

## fh1p
“Yahweh is the one who protects me.” The psalmist speaks of Yahweh as if he were a rock that would protect him. See how this is translated in [Psalms 18:2](../018/002.md). (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 93:intro
## r2fh
See [[PSA-r2fh]]
# 93:1
## gs1h
See [[PSA-gs1h]]
## cif2
The psalmist speaks of Yahweh’s strength and majesty as if they were things Yahweh wears. Alternate translation: “he shows everyone that he is a powerful king” or “his majesty is there for all to see, like the robe a king wears; everything about Yahweh shows that he is strong and he is ready to do great work” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-simile]])

## g76j
the power of a king and the way a king acts

## y5ut
put on a belt—a band of leather or another material that a person wears around his waist—to prepare for work or battle

## nv27
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “You have firmly established the world” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## m76h
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “no one will ever move it” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 93:2
## q45b
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “You established your throne in ancient times” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

## vq1t
“you have always existed”

# 93:3
## u6dq
Some versions read “floods … floods’.” The word often refers to a river (See: “the River” in [Psalms 72:8](../072/008.md)), but “oceans … oceans’” has been chosen here because oceans, not rivers, have “waves” that “crash and roar.”

## qkw5
The psalmist speaks of the ocean as if it were a person who could speak. Alternate translation: “have made a mighty noise because their waves crash and roar” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## kli1
make a long, loud sound.

# 93:4
## bv4p
The phrase “the mighty breakers of the sea” means basically the same thing as “many waves” and emphasizes how great these waves are. Alternate translation: “Above the crashing of all of the very great waves of the sea” (See: [[figs-doublet]])

## x149
large waves coming to land

## z182
The psalmist speaks of where God lives as if it were high above the earth. Alternate translation: “in heaven” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 93:5
## g6vn
very serious

## s6rb
“always stay the same” or “never change”

## f31y
The psalmist speaks of Yahweh’s house as if it were a woman wearing beautiful clothing or jewelry and of Yahweh’s holiness as the beautiful clothing or jewelry. Alternate translation: “your house is beautiful because you are holy” or “your holiness makes your house beautiful the way beautiful clothes and jewelry make a woman more beautiful” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## a9yi
makes beautiful

## xku4
This refers to the temple in Jerusalem.

## lsy7
This idiom refers to the duration of God’s life. Since God lives forever, it can also be translated as forever. Alternate translation: “for as long as you live” or “forever” (See: [[figs-idiom]])

## h3e7

# 94:2
## i9f5
“Come, judge of the earth, and punish the proud”

## sjp9
The psalmist speaks of starting to do something as getting up. If your language has a word for beginning to act, use it here. Alternate translation: “Take action” or “Do something” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 94:3
## dx87
The psalmist repeats the question to show that he is unhappy with how long Yahweh has allowed the wicked to rejoice. This rhetorical question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “You have waited too long Yahweh; you have waited too long to stop the wicked from rejoicing.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## nve7
Why the wicked rejoice can be stated clearly. Alternate translation: “will the wicked rejoice because you never punish them for the evil deeds they do” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 94:4
## h71p
“All those who do evil pour out”

## ks4b
The psalmist writes of the wicked speaking as if their words were a liquid being poured out. (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## dri9
The translation can make explicit that these people boast about their evil actions. Alternate translation: “all those who do evil boast about their evil deeds” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 94:5
## ifj9
Here the metonym “nation” refers to the people of the nation. Alternate translation: “afflict the people of the nation” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## l8he
The psalmist speaks of powerful people treating powerless people badly as if it were crushing them or breaking them in pieces. See how “break in pieces” is translated in [Psalms 72:4](../072/004.md). Alternate translation: “They completely destroy” or “They greatly harm” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ybz4
This rhetorical question emphasizes the anger of the writer with the wicked people he is speaking to. This question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “stop your foolish ways!” or “learn from your mistakes!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 94:9
## b851
These rhetorical questions can be translated as statements or commands. Alternate translation: “God made ears, so he can hear. God made eyes, so he can see.” or “God made ears, so stop acting as if he does not hear. God made eyes, so stop acting as if he does not see.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## p12i

## ig2v
This question can be translated as a statement. It is implied that Yahweh corrects his people. Alternate translation: “You know that Yahweh corrects the nations, so you can be sure that he will correct his people!” or “he will punish his people!” (See: [[figs-rquestion]] and [[figs-explicit]])

## ex2w
This can be stated in positive form. Possible meanings are (1) God corrects. Alternate translation: “he corrects” or (2) God punishes. “he punishes” (See: [[figs-litotes]])

## s3c6

## pgd7
This is a hypothetical situation. Yahweh did help him, so he was not lying in the place of silence. (See: [[figs-hypo]])

## zjq1
Here “would be lying down” refers to “death” and “in the place of silence” refers to the grave.” Alternate translation: “in a short time, I would be dead, lying in a silent grave” (See: [[figs-euphemism]])

## gqn7

## f9kp
The psalmist speaks of care as if he could count separate cares. The abstract noun “consolations” can be translated with the verb “console” or “comfort.” Alternate translation: “When I have been worried about many things, you have comforted me and made me happy” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 96:11
## p26n
Possible meanings are (1) the heavens and the earth are spoken of as if they have emotions like people. Alternate translation: “Let it be as if the heavens are glad and the earth rejoices” or (2) “the heavens” and “the earth” are metonyms for those who dwell in those places. Alternate translation: “Let those who live in the heavens be glad and let those who live on the earth rejoice” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## v2y3
The implied information is that this refers to all the creatures living in the sea. They are spoken of as if they should shout with joy like people might do. Alternate translation: “the sea creatures shout joyfully” (See: [[figs-explicit]] and [[figs-personification]])

# 96:12
## ms99
“Let the fields and all that is in them rejoice.” The psalmist speaks as if “the fields” and the animals that live in them have emotions like people. Alternate translation: “Let it be as if the fields themselves and all the animals that live in them are rejoicing” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## j65h
This speaks about the trees as if they were people who could shout for joy. Alternate translation: “let it be as if all the trees in the forest shout for joy” (See: [[figs-personification]])

# 96:13
## gp2k
The three phrase have similar meanings; the last two phases are used to strengthen the first. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## jp59

## yk8h

## yn8l
The words “he will judge” are understood. Alternate translation: “he will judge the peoples with his faithfulness” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

## z7r2

The word “throne” is a metonym for the deeds and words of the one who sits on it. The psalmist speaks as if righteousness and justice were physical objects that made the throne secure. shakes with fear

## r8fc

## t73s

Alternate translation: “Everyone can see that God is just, the same way that everyone can see the skies” or (2) the skies refer to the beings that live in the heavens. Alternate translation: “All those who live in heaven declare that Yahweh is just” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

## v6ge

# 97:11
## zi9a
These two phrases have similar meanings and are used together for emphasis. Alternate translation: “Yahweh sows light for those who do right, and he sows gladness for those with honest hearts” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-ellipsis]])

## c84h
The word “sown” is a metaphor for actions with future results. “Light” is a metaphor for good things. Alternate translation: “Yahweh plans for good things to happen to the righteous in the future” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## s7rl
The adjective “righteous” can be translated as a noun phrase. Alternate translation: “righteous people” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

## a81f
The words “is sown” are understood. The word “sown” is a metaphor for actions with future results. The abstract noun “gladness” can be stated as “happy.” Alternate translation: “gladness is sown for those with honest hearts” or “Yahweh plans for people with honest hearts to be happy in the future” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## tqu7
The heart is a synecdoche for the whole person. Alternate translation: “honest people” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

## gp6t

## mf5d
shake with fear

## rm8y
You may need to make explicit that the cherubim are those on the lid of the ark of the covenant. The biblical writers often spoke of the ark of the covenant as if it were Yahweh’s footstool upon which he rested his feet as he sat on his throne in heaven above. Alternate translation: “He sits on his throne above the cherubim on the ark of the covenant” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## i5p3
shakes

# 99:2
## w3l7
“Not only is Yahweh great in Zion, he is exalted above all the nations” or “Not only does Yahweh rule in Zion, he rules over all the nations”

## e9l4
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “people in all the nations exalt him” or “people in all nations praise him greatly” (See: [[figs-activepassive]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 99:3
## r44j
Here the author shifts from speaking about God, to speaking to God. After this phrase though, he shifts back to speaking about God.

# 99:4
## c51t
The abstract noun “justice” can be translated using the phrase “what is just.” Alternate translation: “he loves doing what is just” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## f41e
The abstract noun “fairness” is a metonym for the laws that are fair. Alternate translation: “The laws you have established are fair” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 99:5
## j6yj
Here the word “footstool” likely refers to the ark of the covenant, which the biblical writers often spoke of as if it were Yahweh’s footstool upon which he rested his feet as he sat on his throne in heaven above. The translation can make explicit that the people are to worship Yahweh. Alternate translation: “worship Yahweh at his footstool” or “worship Yahweh before his throne in the temple” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

important, given seriously

## y5x4

This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “he made the waves still” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 107:30
## pch1
“to the harbor where they wanted to go”

# 107:31
## bw2u
Here the word “Oh” is used to convey a strong desire for people to praise Yahweh. The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be translated with an adjective. See how you translated this in [Psalms 107:8](../107/008.md). Alternate translation: “Let people praise Yahweh because he is faithful to his covenant” or “People should praise Yahweh because he is faithful to his covenant” (See: [[figs-exclamations]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

## r3mg
“when the elders sit together.” The elders sat together to discuss issues in the community and to make decisions for the community.

## c1mr
Here David is referring to himself by his heart. Also, the word “fixed” means to trust completely. Alternate translation: “My heart is fixed on you, God” or “I am trusting completely in you, God” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]] and [[figs-idiom]])

# 108:4
## ce4i
These two phrases have similar meanings. They speak of the greatness of Yahweh’s covenant faithfulness and trustworthiness as if they were objects that were tall enough to reach up to the sky. The abstract nouns “faithfulness” and “trustworthiness” can be translated as adjectives. Alternate translation: “For your covenant faithfulness and trustworthiness are greater than the distance between heaven and earth” or “For you are more faithful to your covenant and more worthy of people trusting you than the sky is higher than the earth” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 108:5
## y6qh
The psalmist is asking God to show that he is exalted. Being exalted above the heavens represents being great. Alternate translation: “God, show that you are exalted above the heavens” or “God, show that you are great in the heavens” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## v9cz
Here Yahweh is referred to by his “glory.” Alternate translation: “may you be exalted” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 108:6

Here Yahweh’s “right hand” refers to his power. Alternate translation: “by your power” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## m9rg
Answering here represents responding to his request. Alternate translation: “respond to my request” or “answer my prayer” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

Here David describes God speaking something because he is holy as speaking “in his holiness,” as if his holiness were something that he was physically inside of. Alternate translation: “God, because he is holy, has said” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## acw8
God speaks of the tribe of Ephraim as if it were his army. The helmet symbolizes equipment for war. Alternate translation: “Ephraim is like a helmet I have chosen” or “The tribe of Ephraim is my army” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## sj9d
a hard hat that soldiers wear to protect their heads from injury

## yz46
God chose men from the tribe of Judah to be the kings of his people, and he speaks of that tribe as if it were his scepter. Alternate translation: “The tribe of Judah is like my scepter” or “Judah is the tribe through whom I rule my people” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 108:9
## db77
See [[PSA-db77]]
## xz1w
God speaks of Moab being unimportant as if Moab were a washbasin or a lowly servant. Alternate translation: “Moab is like a bowl that I use for washing” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## n1ym
God was probably speaking of taking ownership of Edom as if he were symbolically throwing his shoe onto that land to show that he owns it. However some versions have other interpretations. Alternate translation: “I take ownership of the land of Edom” or “I throw my shoe onto the land of Edom to show that it is mine” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[translate-symaction]])

The grinding of teeth is an expression of extreme anger. See how you translated a similar phrase in [Psalms 35:16](../035/016.md). (See: [[translate-symaction]])

## xx9c
The writer speaks of the wicked person’s eventual death as if that person were something, such as ice, that can melt. Alternate translation: “eventually die” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

## ig26
Possible meanings of “desire” are (1) the emotional desire that wicked people have. Alternate translation: “the things that wicked people want to do will never happen” or (2) it is a metonym for the things that wicked people have desired. Alternate translation: “wicked people will lose the things that they desired” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 113:intro
## p4uj
See [[PSA-p4uj]]
# 113:1
## qx3m
See [[PSA-qx3m]]
## pvw9
Here the word “name” represents Yahweh, himself. Alternate translation: “Yahweh” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 113:3
## fc3w
This phrase refers to the directions east, where the sun rises, and west, where the sun sets. The writer uses these two extremes to represent everywhere on earth. See how you translated this in [Psalms 50:1](../050/001.md). Alternate translation: “Everywhere on earth” (See: [[figs-merism]])

## l5e2
Here the word “name” represents Yahweh, himself. This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “people should praise Yahweh” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-activepassive]])

# 113:4
## s2f9
The glory of God is spoken of as if it was very high. Alternate translation: “his glory is higher than the skies” or “his glory is enormously great” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 113:5
## wih3
This is the first part of a rhetorical question that the writer asks to emphasize that there is no one like Yahweh. It may be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “There is no one like Yahweh our God, who has his seat on high” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## q76s
“who is enthroned on high” or “who rules in the highest position”

# 113:6
## ig92
This is the second part of a rhetorical question that the writer asks to emphasize that there is no one like Yahweh. It may be translated as a statement that begins a new sentence. Alternate translation: “There is no one like Yahweh our God, who looks down at the sky and at the earth.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 113:7
## d8bp
These two phrases are parallel. The writer speaks of Yahweh helping and honoring people who are poor as if Yahweh caused them to stand up from sitting in dirt and ashes. (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## rib6
Sitting in dirt and ashes represents either poverty or despair. (See: [[translate-symaction]])

# 113:8
## ztn3
Here the second phrase clarifies that the first phrase refers to the princes of Yahweh’s people. These two statements can be combined. Alternate translation: “so that Yahweh may seat him next to rulers of his people” (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

# 113:9
## c3e3
Here “home” refers to the children that live in a home. Alternate translation: “He gives children to the woman who had none” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 114:intro
## vu9f
See [[PSA-vu9f]]
# 114:1
## eph8
See [[PSA-eph8]]
## d4dj
These two lines are parallel, with “Israel” and “the house of Jacob” meaning the same thing and “Egypt” and “that foreign people” referring to the same people. (See: [[figs-parallelism]])

## trv6
The word “house” here represents the family or descendents. The verb may be supplied to this phrase. Alternate translation: “the descendants of Jacob left people who spoke a language that the descendants of Jacob did not understand” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-ellipsis]])

# 114:2
## rxv7
Possible meanings for “Judah” and “Israel” are (1) they refer to the land. Alternate translation: “The land of Judah became Yahweh’s holy place, the land of Israel became his kingdom” or (2) they are metonyms for the people of Judah and Israel. Alternate translation: “The people of Judah became those among whom Yahweh lived, the people of Israel became those over whom he ruled” (See: [[figs-metonymy]] and [[figs-ellipsis]])

# 114:3
## pq6x
The writer speaks of the Sea of Reeds as if it were a person who fled away as Yahweh led the people of Israel through on dry ground. Alternate translation: “It was as though the sea looked and fled” (See: [[figs-personification]])

## wht9
The writer speaks of the Jordan River as if it were a person who retreated as Yahweh led the people of Israel through on dry ground. Alternate translation: “it was as though the Jordan retreated” (See: [[figs-personification]])

# 114:4
## hr5q
The writer speaks of the mountains and hills as if they are alive. He is probably speaking of an earthquake when he describes their fear at the presence of Yahweh as if they were jumping like rams and lambs. Alternate translation: “It was as if the mountains shook like jumping rams and the hills shook like jumping lambs” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 114:5
## a5rh
See [[PSA-a5rh]]
# 114:6
## m256
The writer speaks of the mountains and hills as if they are alive. He is probably speaking of an earthquake when he describes their fear at the presence of Yahweh as if they were jumping like rams and lambs. See how you translated similar phrases in [Psalms 114:4](./004.md). Alternate translation: “shake like jumping rams … shake like jumping lambs” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

# 114:7
## xi33
The last two phrases are parallel. The verb may be supplied in the second line. Alternate translation: “Tremble, earth, before the Lord, tremble at the presence of the God of Jacob” (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-ellipsis]])

## k6tn
Possible meanings are (1) the writer is speaking to the earth as if it were a person and commands it to tremble with fear before God or (2) the word “earth” is a metonym for those who live on the earth. Alternate translation: “Tremble, everyone one earth” (See: [[figs-personification]] and [[figs-metonymy]])

# 114:8
## u6n5
These two phrases are parallel. The writer describes Yahweh causing water to flow from the rock as if he turned the rock into water. (See: [[figs-parallelism]] and [[figs-metaphor]])

## ca4j
The verb may be supplied from the previous phrase. Alternate translation: “He turned the hard rock into a spring of water” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]])

# 115:intro
## it4i
See [[PSA-it4i]]
# 115:1
## i2ig
See [[PSA-i2ig]]
## l7ze
The writer repeats the phrase “Not to us” in order to emphasize that they are not worthy to receive the honor that is due only to Yahweh. If necessary, a verbal phrase may be supplied here. Alternate translation: “Do not bring honor to us, Yahweh” (See: [[figs-doublet]] and [[figs-ellipsis]])

## a1ln
The word “us” refers to the people of Israel.

## xrr8
Here the word “name” represents Yahweh, himself. Alternate translation: “but bring honor to yourself” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## ckd9
The abstract noun “faithfulness” can be translated as an adjective. The abstract noun “trustworthiness” can be translated with a verbal phrase. Alternate translation: “because you are faithful to your covenant and you are worthy of people trusting you” (See: [[figs-abstractnouns]])

# 115:2
## frw2
This rhetorical question emphasizes that there should be no reason for the nations to say what they say. This question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “The people of the nations should not be able to say, ‘Where is their God?’” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

## ya4w
The people of other nations use this question to mock the people of Israel and to express that they do not see Yahweh helping them. This question can be translated as a statement. Alternate translation: “Your God is not here to help you.” (See: [[figs-rquestion]])

# 115:4
## ums8
Here the word “hands” represents the people who made the idols. Alternate translation: “things which men have made” (See: [[figs-synecdoche]])

# 115:5
## p4em
The idols do not have real mouths, eyes, ears, or noses. Rather, people made them with the likeness of mouths, eyes, ears, and noses. The writer is emphasizing that these idols are not really alive. You can make clear the implied information. Alternate translation: “People have given mouths to those idols” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 115:7
## n3bn
The idols do not have real hands, feet, or mouths. Rather, people made them with the likeness of hands, feet, and mouths. The writer is emphasizing that these idols are not really alive. You can make this implicit information clear. Alternate translation: “People have given hands to those idols” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## ak88
“but those hands do not feel”

# 115:8
## x5i2
Those who make and worship idols become lifeless and powerless, just like those idols. The full meaning of this statement can be made clear. Alternate translation: “Those who make them become lifeless like they are, as does everyone who trusts in them” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

# 115:9
## tqr6
The word “Israel” represents the people of Israel. Alternate translation: “People of Israel, trust in Yahweh” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## l1tq
The writer speaks of Yahweh as if he were a shield because he protects his people as a shield would protect them from harm. Alternate translation: “the one who helps you and protects you” (See: [[figs-metaphor]])

# 115:10
## fa9s
Here the word “House” represents the family or descendants. This phrase refers to the priests, who were descendants of Aaron. Alternate translation: “Descendants of Aaron” or “Priests” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 115:12
## sbd2
“has paid attention to us”

## bns5
This refers to the people of Israel, who are descendants of Jacob, who also called Israel. Alternate translation: “the people of Israel” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

## bu8h
This refers to the priests, who are descendants of Aaron. Alternate translation: “the descendants of Aaron” or “the priests” (See: [[figs-metonymy]])

# 115:13
## j4vy
“both small and great.” Possible meanings are that this refers (1) to social status or (2) to age. In either case, the two extremes represent every person, regardless of age or social status. (See: [[figs-merism]])

# 115:14
## fg71
The writer is speaking of the number of children that the people of Israel would have. Alternate translation: “May Yahweh increase the number of your children more and more” (See: [[figs-explicit]])

## ezp4
“both your children and your descendants’ children.” The writer clarifies that his request for more children applies not only to the present generation, but to their descendants, as well.

# 115:15
## psd5
This can be stated in active form. Alternate translation: “May Yahweh, who made heaven and earth, bless you” (See: [[figs-activepassive]])

# 115:16
## qu64
This does not mean that the earth does not belong to Yahweh, but that has given the earth to mankind as a dwelling place.

# 115:17
## n4er
The adjective “dead” may be translated with a noun phrase. Alternate translation: “People who are dead” (See: [[figs-nominaladj]])

## m2zu
This parallel phrase has a similar meaning to phrase before it. The verb may be supplied from that previous phrase to make the meaning clear. Alternate translation: “nor do any who go down into silence praise Yahweh” (See: [[figs-ellipsis]] and [[figs-parallelism]])

## hd34
The writer speaks of the grave or the place of the dead as a place of silence where no one can speak. This is a euphemism for death. Alternate translation: “nor do any who go to the place of the dead” (See: [[figs-metaphor]] and [[figs-euphemism]])

# 115:18
## m43y
The word “we” refers to the people of Israel who are still alive.

## f41h
